Tree in a Cemetery: Ownership and Use
Ruling on ownership and use of trees growing in cemeteries

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership and use of a tree in a cemetery for each of three scenarios: pre-existing tree, planted tree, and spontaneously grown tree.

Core Idea
A tree in a cemetery is not automatically public property—its ruling depends on when and how it appeared.

This passage from al-Khaniyyah presents three distinct scenarios for a tree growing in a cemetery. Understanding these nuanced rulings highlights how Hanafi fiqh balances private ownership rights with the sanctity and public benefit of cemetery land.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
Read the passage and note the three distinct cases: tree before cemetery, tree after cemetery with known planter, and tree after cemetery by natural growth.
The scenario of mawat land with no owner is also mentioned but is a sub-case of the first.
2
Determine the owner for each scenario
For each scenario, identify who has ownership rights over the tree. In scenario 1, the original landowner. In scenario 2, the known planter. In scenario 3, the judge has authority.
Note that in scenario 2, ownership is retained but with a charity obligation on the fruit.
3
Identify obligations or restrictions
In scenario 2, the owner must give the price of the fruit in charity. In scenario 3, the judge decides whether to uproot or leave the tree.
The charity obligation stems from the land being dedicated for public benefit.
4
Understand the role of the judge
In scenario 3, the judge has discretion to act in the public interest. This reflects the broader principle of the ruler's authority over public spaces.
The judge may consider factors like whether the tree causes harm or benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخانية : شجرة في مقبرة قالوا: إن كانت نابتة في الأرض قبل أن يجعلها مقبرة فمالك الأرض أحق بها يصنع بها ما شاء، وإن كانت الأرض مواتاً ولا مالك لها فجعلها أهل تلك المحلة أو القرية مقبرة فإن الشجرة وموضعها من الأرض على ما كان حكمها في القديم، وإن نبتت الشجرة بعدما جعلت مقبرة، فإن كان الغارس معلوماً كانت له يتصدق بثمن ثمرها. وإن كانت الشجرة نبتت بنفسها فحكمها يكون للقاضي، إن رأى قلعها أو إبقاءها على المقبرة فعل.
And in al-Khaniyyah: a tree in a cemetery, they said: if it grew in the land before it was made a cemetery, the owner of the land has more right to it; he may do with it what he wishes. And if the land was dead (mawat) with no owner, and the people of that neighbourhood or village made it a cemetery, then the tree and its place in the land are as their ruling in the past. And if the tree grew after it was made a cemetery, then if the planter is known, it belongs to him, and he should give in charity the price of its fruit. And if the tree grew by itself, its ruling belongs to the judge; if he sees fit to uproot it or leave it in the cemetery, he may do so.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage from al-Khaniyyah addresses a tree that exists in a cemetery. The ruling varies based on three scenarios: (1) The tree was present before the land became a cemetery: the original landowner retains full ownership. (2) The tree grew after the land became a cemetery but was planted by a known person: the planter owns it but must give the value of its fruit in charity, because the land is now dedicated for the public benefit. (3) The tree grew spontaneously: the judge decides whether to remove it or leave it, based on public interest.

Key Terms
الموات (al-mawat): Dead land, uncultivated and unowned.
مقبرة (maqbarah): Cemetery, a place dedicated for burial.
غارس (gharis): Planter, one who plants a tree.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The ruling on a tree in a cemetery depends on the timing of its growth and the identity of the planter, with charity required on fruit from a known planter.

Serving Wine to a Non-Muslim Father: Limits of Filial Duty
Ruling statement on the limits of serving a non-Muslim parent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a Muslim child serving wine to his non-Muslim father and identify the conditions under which other acts of service are permitted.

Core Idea
A Muslim child may not assist his non-Muslim father in sin or shirk, but practical kindness like cooking is allowed.

This lesson examines the boundaries of filial duty (birr al-walidayn) when the parent is non-Muslim. The Hanafi school teaches that while kindness is enjoined, it does not extend to actions that violate Islamic law, such as serving wine or facilitating shirk. However, mundane acts of service like lighting a fire for cooking are permitted, provided no haram substance is involved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited acts and their rationale
List the three explicit prohibitions: serving wine, handing the cup, and accompanying to church. The rationale is that these constitute assistance in sin (ithm) or shirk.
The prohibition is based on the principle of 'la ta'awunu 'ala al-ithm' (do not cooperate in sin).
2
Identify the permitted act and its condition
Lighting a fire for cooking is allowed, but only if no carrion (maytah) is in the pot. This shows that the prohibition is specific to sinful acts, not to all forms of service.
Carrion is impure (najis) and its presence would involve handling impurity, which is also prohibited.
3
Extract the underlying legal principle
The Hanafi principle: kindness to parents is limited to lawful matters; one cannot obey a parent in disobedience to Allah. Obedience to parents is not absolute.
This aligns with the hadith: 'No obedience to a creature in disobedience to the Creator.'
4
Apply to similar scenarios
Consider whether the child can buy wine for the father, or carry his chair to church. The principle extends: any act that directly facilitates sin or shirk is prohibited.
Acts of general welfare (e.g., cooking, cleaning) are permitted as long as they do not involve haram elements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يسقي أباه الكافر خمراً ولا يناوله القدح ويأخذه منه ولا يذهب به إلى البيعة ويرده منها، ويوقد تحت قدره إذا لم يكن فيه ميتة.
And he should not give his disbelieving father wine to drink, nor hand him the cup and take it from him, nor go with him to the church and return him from it, and he may light a fire under his cooking pot if there is no carrion in it.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage outlines the limits of a Muslim child's obedience to a non-Muslim parent. The child is prohibited from directly serving wine to the parent, even handing the cup. Additionally, the child should not accompany the parent to a church (bi'ah) for worship. However, in practical matters like lighting a fire for cooking, it is permissible as long as the food does not contain carrion (dead animal). This shows that kindness to parents does not extend to assisting in sin or shirk.

Key Terms
البيعة (al-bi'ah): Church or place of worship of non-Muslims.
الميتة (al-maytah): Carrion, an animal that died without proper Islamic slaughter, considered impure (najis).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The pot must not contain carrion.
C) The child must not be seen by others.
D) The fire must be used only for cooking vegetables.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim child must not assist a non-Muslim parent in any act that is sinful, but may perform neutral acts of service as long as they do not involve haram elements.

Prohibition of Using Gold and Silver Vessels
Hanafi ruling on gold/silver vessels with proof and detailed commentary on usage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that using gold and silver vessels for eating, drinking, applying oil, and perfuming is haram, and explain the basis in the hadith of Hudhayfah and the principle of karahah tahrimiyah.

Core Idea
Using gold or silver vessels for direct consumption is prohibited by explicit hadith, and the prohibition extends to eating, drinking, and similar uses.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on gold and silver vessels, a topic that combines textual proof (dalil naqli) with nuanced discussion on the manner of use. We will see how the Hanafi school derives a prohibition from authentic hadith and how later scholars refined the application to distinguish between direct and indirect use.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Read the matn: 'Eating, drinking, applying oil, and perfuming using gold and silver vessels for both men and women.' The author rules that these acts are prohibited (haram), expressed as 'karahah tahrimiyah'.
The term 'kareeh' in Hanafi fiqh often means haram when supported by strong evidence.
2
Locate the dalil and its wajh al-dalalah
The author cites the hadith of Hudhayfah: 'Do not drink from gold and silver vessels...' The prohibitive form (la + verb) indicates haram. The phrase 'for them in this world and for you in the Hereafter' shows these vessels are exclusively for non-Muslims.
The hadith is mutafaq alayh (agreed upon) and provides decisive textual evidence.
3
Understand the extension to other uses
The author extends the ruling from drinking to eating, applying oil, and perfuming. This is based on qiyas: all these uses involve direct contact with the prohibited material, and the illa is the enjoyment and resemblance to the extravagant.
The hadith of Umm Salamah about drinking from silver vessels gurgling Hellfire reinforces the severity.
4
Analyze the nuance on direct vs indirect use
The commentary discusses differences on whether the prohibition applies only when the vessel is used directly (e.g., drinking from the cup) or also when one takes food from the vessel with the hand or a spoon. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that initial customary use (ibtida' al-istimal) determines prohibition.
This nuance shows the depth of Hanafi legal reasoning in applying general texts to specific scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( والأكل والشرب والإدهان والتطيب في إناء ذهب وفضة للرجال والنساء لما روى حذيفة أنه قال : سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول «لا تلبسوا الحرير ولا الديباج ولا تشربوا في آنية الذهب والفضة ولا تأكلوا في صحافها وإنها لهم في الدنيا ولكم في الآخرة» (۱) رواه البخاري ومسلم وأحمد. وروي عن أم سلمة عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال إن الذي يشرب في إناء الفضة إنما يجرجر في بطنه نار جهنم»(۱) فإذا ثبت في الشرب فالأكل كذلك والتطيب لاستوائهم في الاستعمال فيكون الوارد فيها يكون وارداً فيما هو في معناها دلالة، ولأنها تنعم بتنعم المترفهين والمسرفين وتشبه بهم وقد قال الله تعالى فيهم واذهبتم طيباتكم في حياتكم الدنيا [الأحقاف : ۲۰] وقال عليه الصلاة والسلام «من تشبه بقوم فهو منهم (۲) والمراد بقوله كره كراهة التحريم ويستوي فيه الرجال والنساء لإطلاق ما روينا، وكذا الأكل بمعلقة الذهب والفضة والاكتحال بميلها وما أشبه ذلك الاستعمالات. ومعنى يجرجر يردد من جرجر الفحل إذا ردد صوته في حنجرته. قال في النهاية : قيل صورة الإدهان المحرم هو أن يأخذ آنية الذهب أو الفضة ويصب الدهن على الرأس، أما إذا أدخل يده وأخذ الدهن ثم يصب على الرأس لا يكره، وعزاه إلى الذخيرة. وظاهر عبارة النهاية حيث عبر بقيل أنه ضعيف قال في الجامع الصغير قالوا: وهذا إذا كان يصب من الآنية على رأسه أم بدنه أما إذا أدخل يده في الإناء وأخرج منها الدهن ثم استعمل فلا يكره اهـ وهو يفيد صحته قال في العتابية وأرى أنه مخالف لما ذكره المصنف في المكحلة والميل ولا بد أن ينفصل عنها حين الاكتحال ومع ذلك فقد ذكر في المحرمات. واعترض صاحب التسهيل على ما قيل في صورة الإدهان وهو يقتضي أنه لا يكره إذا أخذ الطعام من آنية الذهب والفضة بمعلقة ثم أكل منها، وكذا إذا أخذ بيده ثم أكل منها. وأجاب عنه صاحب الدرر والغرر بما يصلح جواباً عما أورده صاحب العناية قال حيث قال بعد ذكر الاعتراض أقول : منشؤه الغفلة عن معنى عبارة المشايخ وعدم الوقوف على مرادهم؛ أما الأول فلأن «من» في قولهم من إناء ذهب ابتدائية ، وأما الثالث فلأن مرادهم أن الأدوات المصنوعة من المحرمات إنما يحرم استعمالها فيما صنعت له بحسب متعارف الناس فإن الأواني الكبيرة المصوغة من الذهب والفضة لأجل أكل الطعام إنما يحرم استعمالها إذا أكل منها باليد أو المعلقة، وأما إذا أخذ منها ووضع على موضع مباح فأكل منه لم يحرم لانتفاء ابتداء الاستعمال منها، وكذا الأواني الصغيرة المصنوعة لأجل الادهان ونحوه إنما يحرم استعمالها إذا أخذت وصب منها الدهن على الرأس لأنها صنعت لأجل الادمان منها بذلك الوجه . وأما إذا أدخل يده وأخذ الدهن وصبه على الرأس من اليد فلا يكره لانتفاء ابتداء الاستعمال منها، فظهر أن مرادهم أن يكون ابتداء الاستعمال المتعارف من ذلك على العرف وأورد عليه بأن الموجود في عبارة المتقدمين كالجامع الصغير والمحيط والذخيرة
He (the author) said: (Eating, drinking, applying oil, and perfuming using gold and silver vessels for both men and women) due to what Hudhayfah narrated: He said: I heard the Messenger of Allah ﷺ say: 'Do not wear silk and brocade, do not drink from gold and silver vessels, and do not eat from their plates, for these are for them in this world and for you in the Hereafter.' Narrated by Bukhari, Muslim, and Ahmad. And it is narrated from Umm Salamah from the Prophet ﷺ that he said: 'Indeed, the one who drinks from a silver vessel is only gurgling Hellfire into his belly.' So if this is established for drinking, then the same applies to eating and applying perfume because they are equal in usage, so what is reported about one is reported about what is in its meaning by implication. And because it is enjoyment like the enjoyment of the extravagant and wasteful and resembles them, and Allah said concerning them: 'And you took your pleasures in your worldly life' [al-Ahqaf: 20], and the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Whoever imitates a people is one of them.' The meaning of 'disliked' here is prohibited (karahah tahrimiyah), and men and women are equal due to the generality of what we narrated. Similarly, eating with a gold or silver spoon and applying kohl with a kohl stick made of them and similar uses. The meaning of 'yujarjir' is to repeat, from the camel vibrating its voice in its throat. In al-Nihayah it is said: The form of prohibited oil application is that one takes a gold or silver vessel and pours oil onto the head. But if he inserts his hand, takes the oil, then pours it onto the head, it is not disliked, and he attributes this to al-Dhakhirah. The apparent wording of al-Nihayah indicates this is weak. In al-Jami' al-Saghir they said: This is if he pours from the vessel onto his head or body; but if he inserts his hand into the vessel and extracts the oil and then uses it, it is not disliked. This indicates its soundness. In al-Atabiyya he said: I see that this contradicts what the author mentioned about the kohl container and the kohl stick, and he must separate from them during application, yet he included them in prohibitions. The author of al-Tashil objected to what was said about the form of oil application, implying that it is not disliked if one takes food from a gold or silver vessel with a spoon and then eats from it, and similarly if he takes it with his hand and then eats from it. The author of al-Durar wa al-Ghurar answered him in a way that suffices as a response to what the author of al-Inayah raised, saying: After mentioning the objection, I say: Its origin is neglect of the meaning of the expression of the scholars and failure to grasp their intent. As for the first, 'min' in their saying 'from a gold vessel' indicates the starting point. As for the third, they meant that tools made from prohibitions are only prohibited to use for the purpose for which they are customarily made; large vessels made of gold and silver for eating food are only prohibited if one eats directly from them by hand or spoon. But if one takes from them and places on a permissible place and then eats, it is not prohibited because the initial use from the vessel is absent. Similarly, small vessels made for oil application and the like are only prohibited if one takes them and pours the oil onto the head because they are made for that purpose. But if one inserts his hand, takes the oil, and pours it on the head from his hand, it is not disliked because the initial use from the vessel is absent. It thus appears that their intent is that the initial customary usage must be from the vessel. An objection was raised that in the expressions of the early scholars like al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Muhit, and al-Dhakhirah, the prohibition is more general.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage is the author's ruling and commentary on using gold and silver vessels. It begins with the matn stating the ruling: eating, drinking, applying oil, and perfuming using gold and silver vessels is prohibited for both men and women. The proof is the hadith of Hudhayfah (narrated by Bukhari, Muslim, Ahmad) which explicitly forbids drinking from gold and silver vessels and eating from their plates, and states that these are for non-believers in this world. Another hadith from Umm Salamah says the one who drinks from a silver vessel gurgles Hellfire. The author argues that by implication, the same ruling applies to eating and perfuming because they are similar uses. Additional proofs include the Quranic verse condemning those who enjoy their luxuries and the hadith 'Whoever imitates a people is one of them.' The author clarifies that 'disliked' here means prohibition (karahah tahrimiyah). The ruling applies equally to men and women. The commentary then discusses specific cases: using gold/silver spoons, kohl sticks, etc. There is a difference of opinion on how the prohibition applies: if one inserts his hand into the vessel and removes oil/food, the initial use from the vessel is absent, so it is not disliked according to some. But the author of al-Durar wa al-Ghurar defends the view that the prohibition depends on the initial customary usage from the vessel. The passage explains the meaning of 'yujarjir' and references works like al-Nihayah, al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Atabiyya, etc.

Key Terms
كراهة التحريم (karāhat al-taḥrīm): A ruling that is prohibited (haram) but expressed as 'disliked' (makruh) in the Hanafi terminology, meaning it is forbidden based on a zanni (non-definitive) evidence.
يجرجر (yujarjir): To gurgle or produce a repeated sound in the throat, used here metaphorically for the sound of Hellfire entering the belly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, what is the status of eating with a gold spoon?
A) Permissible because the spoon is not a vessel
B) Prohibited (haram) like eating from a gold plate
C) Disliked but not haram
D) Permissible only if the spoon is not used directly

Key Takeaway
Using gold and silver vessels for direct consumption is haram based on explicit hadith, and the Hanafi school treats this as prohibitory dislike (karahah tahrimiyah) with nuanced application to indirect use.

Acceptance of Reports from a Fasiq in Mu'amalat and the Condition of Tahrri
Establishes ruling on accepting reports from fasiq in mu'amalat with tahrri

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting reports from a fasiq in mu'amalat, explain the requirement of tahrri, and distinguish between mu'amalat and diyanat with examples.

Core Idea
A fasiq's report is accepted in transactions after diligent investigation, but not in purely religious matters.

This lesson examines the nuanced Hanafi position on accepting reports from an immoral person (fasiq) in financial transactions versus purely religious matters. While the Quran commands investigation when a fasiq brings news, the Hanafi school applies this with distinction, accepting such reports in mu'amalat after tahrri to avoid hardship, but rejecting them in diyanat. Through this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, we will explore the proof, conditions, and practical implications of this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its distinction
State the Hanafi position: the report of a fasiq is accepted in mu'amalat but not in diyanat. Read the opening line of the passage to see this distinction clearly.
The passage begins with 'والفاسق في المعاملات لا في الديانات'.
2
Examine the Quranic proof
Locate the verse cited (49:6) and explain how the Hanafis derive the requirement of tahrri. The command 'fata bayyanu' indicates investigation, but its application is restricted to diyanat by the context; in mu'amalat, after tahrri, acceptance is allowed.
The verse is a general command, but the Hanafis use istihsan to limit it.
3
Analyze the examples given
Consider the cases of the discerning child (mumayyiz) and the slave reporting a gift. What do these tell us about the scope of mu'amalat and the role of tahrri?
The examples show that even in mu'amalat, tahrri may be required or not depending on the narrator.
4
Explore the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on tahrri for the mumayyiz
Note the disagreement: some accept the mumayyiz's report without tahrri, others require it. This reflects the flexibility within the school.
Al-Sarakhsi and Muhammad al-Shaybani are cited for the requirement.
5
Differentiate for the dhimmi
Why is tahrri only recommended (not obligatory) for the dhimmi? The underlying rationale is the assumption of enmity, making lying more likely.
For the fasiq, tahrri is obligatory because truth and falsehood are equally possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكراهية والفاسق في المعاملات لا في الديانات ولو أخبر مسلم ثقة حراً أو عبداً ذكراً أو أنثى ذكرنا حتى إذا قال المميز أهدي إليك فلان هذه الجارية أو بعثني مولاي بها إليك وسعه الأخذ والاستعمال حتى جاز له الوطء بذلك لأن الديانات دخلت تبعاً للمعاملات كما تقدم بخلاف الديانات المقصودة لأنه لا يكثر وقوعها كالمعاملات ولا حرج في اشتراط العدالة ولا حاجة إلى قبول قول الفاسق لأنه متهم فيها، وكذا الكافر والصغير لأنهما متهمان فيها. وأطلق في الهدية والإذن فشمل ما إذا أخبر بإهداء المولى نفسه أو غيره بأن يقول أهداني إليك سيدي، وشمل أيضاً ما إذا أخبر المملوك بإهداء الجواري والمتاع وغيره ؛ كذا في الهداية وغيرها. وفي المحيط : والمعتوه كالصبي ا.هـ. قال في الهداية وفي الإذن بأن جعل المولى عبده مأذوناً له في التجارة قال : لو أن رجلاً قد علم أن جارية لرجل يدعيها رجل فرآها في يد رجل آخر يبيعها فقال الذي في يده الجارية قد كانت كما قلت إلا أنها لي وصدقه في ذلك وكان مسلماً ثقة فلا بأس بأن يشتريها منه . وفي الخانية : ولا تقبل هدية ولا صدقة حتى يتحرى، فإن وقع في قلبه أنه صادق يقبل منه ، وإن لم يقع تحريه على شيء من ذلك بقي ما كان على ما كان، وإن كان وقع تحريه على أنه كاذب لا يقبل منه . قال في التلويح : قيل ذكر فخر الإسلام أن خبر المميز الغير العدل يقبل في الوكالة والهدايا من غير تحر، وفي موضع آخر أنه يشترط التحري وهو المذكور من كلام السرخسي ومحمد فقيل يجوز أن يكون المذكور في كتاب الاستحسان تفسير الهدية فيشترط ويجوز أن يشترط استحساناً، ويجوز أن يكون في المسألة روايتان. قال رحمه الله : والفاسق في المعاملات لا في الديانات يعني يقبل قول الفاسق فيما ذكر لقول تعالى يا أيها الذين آمنوا إن جاءكم فاسق بنبا فتبينوا [الحجرات : ٦] والتبيين وهو طلب البيان وذلك بالتحري وطلب الصدق في خبره لأن الفاسق قد يكون ذا مروءة فيستنكف عن الكذب وقد يكون ذا خسة لا يبالي عن الكذب فوجب طلب التحري، فإن وقع تحريه على أنه صادق يقبل قوله وإلا فلا والأحوط والأوثق أن يريقه ويتيمم. وفي المحيط : ولو أخبر بذلك فاسق أو من لا تعرف عدالته فإن غلب على ظنه صدقه قد قوله وإلا فلا ا هـ . ولا يقبل قول الذمي. وفي الخانية : أي لأن الكافر يعتقد أن المسلم على دين باطل فيقصد الإضرار به للعداوة فترجح الكذب في خبره فلا يجب التحري بل يستحب لأن احتمال الصدق قائم بخلاف ما لو أخبره فاسق فإن التحري يجب لاستواء الصدق والكذب فيه ; كذا في المحيط .
Chapter of Disliked Acts. The immoral person [fasiq] in transactions [mu'amalat] not in religious matters [diyanat]. If a trustworthy Muslim informs, free or slave, male or female, we have mentioned, even if a discerning child [mumayyiz] says: 'So-and-so gifted you this slave-girl' or 'my master sent me to you with her', it is permissible for him to take and use [her] and even to have intercourse with her, because religious matters enter as subsidiary to transactions as previously, unlike purely religious matters because they do not occur frequently like transactions, and there is no difficulty in stipulating integrity, and there is no need to accept the word of the fasiq because he is accused therein, and similarly the disbeliever and the minor because they are accused therein. The statement is absolute regarding gift and permission, so it includes if he reports about the gift of the master himself or others by saying 'my master gifted me to you', and also includes if the slave reports about gifting slave-girls and goods and other than that; thus in al-Hidayah and others. And in al-Muhit: The insane person is like the minor; end. He said in al-Hidayah regarding permission: If a man makes his slave permitted in trade, he said: If a man knows that a slave-girl belongs to a man, another man claims her, he sees her in the hand of another man selling her, and the one in whose hand the slave-girl is says: 'She was as you said except that she is mine', and he confirms him in that and he was a trustworthy Muslim, there is no harm in him buying her from him. And in al-Khaniyyah: A gift or charity is not accepted until he investigates, and if it occurs in his heart that he is truthful, he accepts from him; if his investigation does not fall on anything of that, it remains as it was; if his investigation falls on that he is lying, he does not accept from him. He said in al-Talwih: It was said that Fakhr al-Islam mentioned that the report of a non-just discerning child is accepted in agency and gifts without investigation, and in another place that investigation is stipulated, and this is what is mentioned from the speech of al-Sarakhsi and Muhammad, so it is said that it is possible that what is mentioned in the book of istihsan is an interpretation of gift, so it is stipulated, and it is possible that it is stipulated istihsan, and it is possible that there are two narrations in the issue. The author said: 'And the fasiq in transactions not in religious matters' means the word of the fasiq is accepted in what was mentioned, due to the statement of the Exalted: 'O you who believe, if a fasiq comes to you with news, investigate...' [49:6], and investigation is seeking clarification, and that is through scrutiny and seeking truth in his report, because the fasiq may be chivalrous and refrain from lying, or may be base and not care about lying, so scrutiny must be sought. If his scrutiny falls on that he is truthful, his word is accepted; otherwise no. And the most cautious and reliable is to pour it out and perform tayammum. And in al-Muhit: If a fasiq or one whose probity is unknown informs of that, then if his assumption of his truthfulness predominates, his word is accepted; otherwise no; end. And the word of a dhimmi is not accepted. And in al-Khaniyyah: That is because the disbeliever believes the Muslim is on a false religion, so he intends to harm him due to enmity, so lying predominates in his report, so investigation is not obligatory but recommended because the possibility of truth remains, unlike if a fasiq informs him, for investigation is obligatory due to the equal possibility of truth and falsehood in it; thus in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Quran, explaining the acceptance of reports from a fasiq (immoral person) in financial transactions (mu'amalat) but not in purely religious matters (diyanat). The author begins by stating the general rule: the report of a fasiq—who is not trustworthy in religious matters—is accepted in transactions. This is because transactions occur frequently and requiring strict probity would cause hardship. However, even in transactions, the report must be investigated (tahrri) to determine truthfulness, as indicated by the Quranic verse (49:6) which commands 'investigate' when a fasiq brings news. The passage then gives examples: a discerning child (mumayyiz) or a slave reporting about a gift from their master is accepted, even for intercourse with a slave-girl, because such gifts are part of transactions. The text also discusses the case of a slave permitted in trade, and then delves into the condition of tahrri: if the listener's heart inclines to truth, he accepts; otherwise not. For a dhimmi disbeliever, tahrri is not obligatory but recommended because of inherent enmity. The ruling emphasizes that the standard for accepting reports differs between mu'amalat and diyanat.

Key Terms
فاسق (fasiq): A Muslim who commits major sins or persists in minor sins, lacking probity ('adala).
المعاملات (al-mu'amalat): Financial transactions and dealings, such as buying, selling, gifts, and agency.
الديانات (al-diyanat): Purely religious matters such as rulings of halal and haram without financial consequence.
التحري (al-tahrri): Diligent investigation to determine the truthfulness of a report, based on outward signs and personal conviction.
مميز (mumayyiz): A child who has reached the age of discernment (typically around 7 years) and can understand basic transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, what must be done before accepting a report from a fasiq in a transaction?
A) Obtain two witnesses
B) Perform investigation (tahrri)
C) Require the fasiq to repent
D) Reject it outright

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows accepting a fasiq's report in mu'amalat after tahrri, distinguishing it from diyanat where it is not accepted due to the greater need for certainty.

Specifying the General Invitation Command When Sin Is Present
Hypothetical objection and answer regarding invitation acceptance when sin is present.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the general command to accept invitations is specified when sin is present, and apply the ruling to a role model.

Core Idea
A general command is specified by texts requiring avoidance of sin, especially for role models.

The principle that a general command (al-amm) can be specified (makhsus) by other texts is illustrated in the context of accepting invitations. Even though a hadith may command accepting all invitations, it is qualified when the gathering involves sinful innovation (bid'ah). This lesson examines the Hanafi reasoning for this specification, particularly for those who are exemplary figures (qudwah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling and the objection
The text begins with the ruling: if a person knows beforehand about sin in a gathering, he should not accept. Then an objection is raised: the hadith (al-ḥadīth al-madhkūr) uses the definite article in 'al-da'wah' implying generality, so it covers all invitations, including those with sin.
The objection relies on the usul principle that alif-lam for istighrāq indicates universal inclusion.
2
Understand the specification argument
The answer is that the generality is specified (makhsūs) by other texts that command avoidance of sin, especially for those who are role models (qudwah). Thus, the general command is overridden when doing so would lead to disgrace of the religion (shayn al-dīn) and opening the door to sin.
This is an example of takhsīs by a stronger dalīl.
3
Recognize the role of qudwah (role model)
The specification is especially emphasized for those who are followed. If such a person cannot prevent the sin, he must leave and not sit, because his presence would encourage others to sin.
The anecdote about Imam Abu Hanifa indicates that before he became a qudwah, the ruling may have been different.
4
Apply the ruling to the scenario
Given the specification, the ruling for the qudwah is: do not accept the invitation if you know about the sin beforehand. If you attend and discover it, you must leave if you cannot stop it.
The mufta bihi position is to leave the gathering to avoid dishonor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان بعد الحضور، ولو علم قبل الحضور لا يقبله ولقائل أن يقول الحديث المذكور يشمل ما بعد الحضور وما قبله لأنه قد تقرر في الأصول أن المعرف بالألف واللام إذا لم تكن للعهد الخارجي فهو للاستغراق فيعم كل دعوة، وقد يجاب عنه بأنه وإن كان عاماً فهو مخصوص بالنصوص الدالة على وجوب الاجتناب عن اقتراب تلك البدع ا ممن يقتدى به فلم يقدر على منعهم خرج ولم يقعد لأن في ذلك شين الدين وفتح باب المعصية على المسلمين، وما حكي أن الإمام وقع له ذلك كان قبل أن يصير قدوة.
If he was present afterwards, and if he knew before attending he does not accept it. Someone may say the mentioned hadith includes both before and after attendance because it is established in usul that the definite article with 'al' when not external specification indicates generality, so it includes every invitation. It is answered that although it is general, it is specified by texts indicating the obligation of avoiding those innovations especially for those who are followed as leaders. So if he is unable to prevent them, he leaves and does not sit because that brings shame to the religion and opens the door of sin upon Muslims. What is narrated about the Imam (Abu Hanifa) happening to him was before he became a leader to be followed.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is a ruling discussion on accepting invitations to gatherings where sinful activities (such as innovations) take place. The text presents a hypothetical objection: since the general ruling on accepting invitations (based on hadith) includes all invitations, why should one decline? The answer is that the general command is specified by other texts that require avoiding proximity to sin, especially for those who are exemplary figures. If a person cannot prevent the sin, he must leave and not sit, as staying would dishonor the religion. The anecdote about Imam Abu Hanifa indicates this ruling applies particularly to public figures, as before his prominence he might have attended, but afterward he refrained.

Key Terms
البدع (al-bida'): Innovations in religious matters that contradict the Sunnah
القدوة (al-qudwah): A role model or leader whose actions are followed by others

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the general command to accept invitations specified?
A) Because the hadith is weak.
B) Because of texts requiring avoidance of sin, especially for role models.
C) Because invitations after attendance are not covered.
D) Because the Imam narrated something different.

Key Takeaway
A general command can be specified by stronger texts, and for role models, avoiding sin takes precedence over accepting invitations.

Ruling on Musical Instruments and Singing
Ruling that musical instruments are haram and differences on singing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on musical instruments and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on singing without instruments.

Core Idea
All musical instruments are haram; singing without instruments is disputed among Hanafi scholars.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq first establishes the clear prohibition of musical instruments using two hadiths. It then delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute on singing without accompaniment (al-taghanni al-mujarrad), presenting three views. Understanding this ikhtilaf is essential for issuing nuanced fatwa in contexts where music and singing are prevalent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on instruments
Note that the passage begins by stating that all musical instruments (al-malahi) are haram. This is supported by two hadiths that warn against making them halal.
The ruling is unambiguous for instruments.
2
Examine the evidence (dalil)
The first hadith quotes the Prophet: 'There will be people from my ummah who make lawful (the use of) the private part, silk, wine, and musical instruments.' The second adds a threat of punishment. Identify that the key term 'المعازف' (musical instruments) triggers the prohibition.
Both hadiths are from Bukhari, giving them strong authenticity.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute on singing
After ruling on instruments, the passage shifts to singing without instruments. Find the three positions: absolute prohibition (Shaykh al-Islam), permissibility for linguistic benefit, and permissibility to dispel loneliness (al-Sarakhsi).
The dispute is among Hanafi scholars, not between madhhabs.
4
Analyze the basis for each position
The prohibitionists rely on the generality of the hadith. Those permitting cite understanding meanings/eloquence or dispelling loneliness based on reports from Companions. Note the different interpretive approaches.
Al-Sarakhsi's view is explicitly based on narrations from some Companions.
5
Apply the further rulings on poetry
The passage adds that poetry with wisdom or story is not makruh. Mentioning a dead woman is okay, but a living woman is disliked. These are specific elaborations of the broader ruling.
These rulings show case-by-case application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودلت المسألة على أن الملاهي كلها حرام حتى التغني بضرب القصب قال عليه الصلاة والسلام ليكونن من أمتي أقوام يستحلون الحر والحرير والخمر والمعازف»(۱) أخرجه البخاري. وفي لفظ آخر «ليشربن اناس من أمتي الخمر يسمونها بغير اسمها يعزف على رؤوسهم بالمعازف والمغنيات يخسف الله الأرض ويجعل منهم القرد والخنازير ) (۲) واختلفوا في التغني المجرد قال بعضهم إنه حرام مطلقاً والاستماع إليه معصية لإطلاق الحديث وهو اختيار شيخ الإسلام، ومنهم من قال لا بأس به لیستفید به فهم المعاني والفصاحة، ومنهم من جوز التغني لدفع الوحشة إذا كان وحده ولا يكون على سبيل اللهو وإليه ذهب شمس الأئمة السرخسي لأنه روي بعض الصحابة. ولو كان في الشعر حكم أو قصة لا يكره. وكذا لو كان فيه ذكر امرأة غير معينة، وكذا لو كانت معينة وهي ميتة ولو كانت حية يكره؛ كذا في الشارح.
And this issue indicates that all musical instruments are forbidden, even singing with a reed flute. The Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'There will be people from my ummah who make lawful (the use of) the private part, silk, wine, and musical instruments.' Al-Bukhari related it. In another wording: 'People from my ummah will drink wine, calling it by other names, and musical instruments and female singers will be played over their heads; Allah will cause the earth to swallow them and make them apes and swine.' And they disagreed regarding mere singing (without instruments). Some said it is absolutely forbidden and listening to it is a sin due to the generality of the hadith, and this is the choice of Shaykh al-Islam. Some said there is no harm in it to gain understanding of meanings and eloquence. And some permitted it to dispel loneliness when alone and not for amusement; this is the view of Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi because it has been narrated from some Companions. And if the poetry contains a ruling or a story, it is not disliked. Likewise if it mentions an unspecified woman, and likewise if it specifies a woman who is dead; but if she is living, it is disliked. This is from al-Sharh.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage first establishes that all musical instruments are haram based on two hadiths that warn of punishment for those who consider them permissible. Then it presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on singing without instruments: some (like Shaykh al-Islam) hold it haram absolutely; others permit it for linguistic benefit; and Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi permits it to dispel loneliness. The passage then gives further rulings on poetry: if it contains wisdom or a story, it is not makruh; mentioning a dead woman is okay, but a living woman is disliked.

Key Terms
الملاهي (al-malahi): Musical instruments and activities that distract from Allah
التغني المجرد (al-taghanni al-mujarrad): Singing without musical accompaniment

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which view of Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi is mentioned regarding singing?
A) Singing is absolutely haram.
B) Singing is permissible to dispel loneliness when not for amusement.
C) Singing is permissible only at weddings.
D) Singing with instruments is halal.

Key Takeaway
While all musical instruments are haram, the permissibility of singing without accompaniment is subject to legitimate intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Ruling on Playing Chess and Backgammon
Ruling that chess and backgammon are disliked

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on playing chess and backgammon, cite the hadith 'All play is haram except...', and explain why it is makruh rather than haram.

Core Idea
Chess and backgammon are makruh because the general prohibition of play is overridden by permissibility in principle, but they remain disliked due to potential distraction.

This lesson focuses on the Hanafi view on board games like chess and backgammon. The source from al-Muhit declares them Makruh based on a hadith that forbids all play except specific activities. We will unpack why they are not outright haram and how this ruling applies to daily life.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their ruling
Chess and backgammon are explicitly stated as 'makruh' (disliked) in the Hanafi school. Note that the passage does not use 'haram' for these games.
The term 'يكره' is used, indicating dislike, not prohibition.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The hadith 'All play is haram except a man's playing with his wife, his bow, and his horse' is cited. This is a general prohibition with specific exceptions.
The hadith is ahad (single-transmitter), so its authority is zanni, not qati.
3
Understand the exception and its implication
The exceptions show that not all play is inherently haram. Chess and backgammon fall outside these exceptions, so they remain under the general prohibition, but the Hanafi school does not apply it as absolute haram.
The ruling is modified by istihsan or consideration of need.
4
Note the reasoning behind makruh
The passage explains that such games 'distract from the congregation and group prayers' and 'cause foul speech'. This indicates the ratio legis (illah) for disapproval.
If the distraction leads to neglect of obligatory acts, the ruling may escalate to haram.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط : ويكره اللعب بالشطرنج والنرد والأربعة لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام كل لعب حرام إلا ملاعبة الرجل زوجته وقوسه وفرسه لأنه يصد عن الجمع والجماعات وسبب للوقوع في فواحش الكلام وغيره واستماع صوت الملاهي حرام كالضرب بالقصب وغيره قال عليه الصلاة والسلام استماع الملاهي معصية والجلوس عليها فسق والتلذذ بها كفر» وهذا خرج على وجه التشديد لا أنه يكفر.
And in al-Muhit: It is disliked to play chess, backgammon, and [fourteen?] because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'All play is forbidden except a man's playing with his wife, his bow, and his horse.' Because it distracts from the congregation and group prayers and is a cause for falling into foul speech and other things. And listening to the sound of musical instruments is forbidden, like beating with a reed and other things. He said, peace be upon him: 'Listening to musical instruments is a sin, sitting at them is transgression, and deriving pleasure from them is disbelief.' This is stated in the way of severity, not that it actually makes one a disbeliever.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage from al-Muhit states that playing chess, backgammon, and a game called 'fourteen' (maybe a dice game) is makruh (disliked). The hadith indicates all play is haram except with wife, bow, and horse, because such play distracts from obligations. Listening to musical instruments is explicitly haram, and a severe hadith categorizes it as sin, transgression, and even 'kufr' in a metaphorical sense. The passage clarifies that this is hyperbolic emphasis, not actual disbelief.

Key Terms
الشطرنج (al-shatranj): Chess, a board game
النرد (al-nard): Backgammon, a dice game

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited in the passage, which of the following activities is an exception to the general prohibition of play?
A) Playing chess with one's spouse.
B) Playing with one's bow and horse.
C) Playing backgammon for entertainment.
D) Playing any game in the mosque.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, chess and backgammon are disliked (makruh) but not forbidden, as long as they do not cause neglect of religious duties.

Ruling on Listening to Musical Instruments
Ruling that listening to musical instruments is haram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on listening to musical instruments, explain the hadith that calls it 'kufr', and clarify that this is a severe warning, not actual disbelief.

Core Idea
Listening to musical instruments is haram; the hadith describing it as 'kufr' is hyperbolic emphasis, not takfir.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on music. The passage explicitly states that listening to musical instruments is haram and cites a hadith with extremely strong language. We will see how scholars interpret this as rhetorical emphasis to denote the severity of the sin, not to declare the listener a disbeliever.

Guided Path
1
Identify the forbidden act
The passage states that 'listening to the sound of musical instruments' (استماع صوت الملاهي) is haram. The example given is 'beating with a reed' (ضرب بالقصب).
This includes any form of instrumental music, not necessarily singing.
2
Locate the evidence
A hadith is cited: 'Listening to musical instruments is a sin (معصية), sitting at them is transgression (فسق), and deriving pleasure from them is disbelief (كفر).'
The hadith is used to support the prohibition.
3
Analyze the severe wording
The term 'kufr' is used, but the author clarifies: 'This is stated in the way of severity, not that it actually makes one a disbeliever.'
This is a common rhetorical device in Islamic texts to emphasize the gravity of a sin without literal takfir.
4
Understand the scope of prohibition
The prohibition applies even if one does not actively listen but sits at the gathering. Deriving pleasure from it is even more severely censured.
The hierarchy: listening = sin; sitting = transgression; deriving pleasure = hyperbole for disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط : ويكره اللعب بالشطرنج والنرد والأربعة لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام كل لعب حرام إلا ملاعبة الرجل زوجته وقوسه وفرسه لأنه يصد عن الجمع والجماعات وسبب للوقوع في فواحش الكلام وغيره واستماع صوت الملاهي حرام كالضرب بالقصب وغيره قال عليه الصلاة والسلام استماع الملاهي معصية والجلوس عليها فسق والتلذذ بها كفر» وهذا خرج على وجه التشديد لا أنه يكفر.
And in al-Muhit: It is disliked to play chess, backgammon, and [fourteen?] because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'All play is forbidden except a man's playing with his wife, his bow, and his horse.' Because it distracts from the congregation and group prayers and is a cause for falling into foul speech and other things. And listening to the sound of musical instruments is forbidden, like beating with a reed and other things. He said, peace be upon him: 'Listening to musical instruments is a sin, sitting at them is transgression, and deriving pleasure from them is disbelief.' This is stated in the way of severity, not that it actually makes one a disbeliever.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage from al-Muhit states that playing chess, backgammon, and a game called 'fourteen' (maybe a dice game) is makruh (disliked). The hadith indicates all play is haram except with wife, bow, and horse, because such play distracts from obligations. Listening to musical instruments is explicitly haram, and a severe hadith categorizes it as sin, transgression, and even 'kufr' in a metaphorical sense. The passage clarifies that this is hyperbolic emphasis, not actual disbelief.

Key Terms
الملاهي (al-malahi): Musical instruments, instruments of divertissement
تشديد (tashdid): Severity, hyperbole in warning

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the author explain the hadith that calls deriving pleasure from music 'kufr'?
A) It is literal disbelief.
B) It is a severe warning, not actual disbelief.
C) It applies only to those who defend music.
D) It is abrogated by later revelation.

Key Takeaway
Listening to musical instruments is haram in the Hanafi school, and the strong language of 'kufr' in the hadith is a rhetorical device emphasizing the gravity of the sin.

Silk for Men: Prohibition, Exception, and Use as Pillow
Ruling on silk: prohibition for men with exception, and permissibility of use as pillow with Maliki dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on men wearing silk, explain the four-finger exception, and distinguish between wearing and using silk as a pillow, including the Maliki disagreement.

Core Idea
Silk is haram for men to wear, but a small patch (≤4 fingers) is excused; using silk as a pillow is permissible.

The author begins the chapter on clothing by discussing the precedence of eating and drinking over dress. He then delivers a clear ruling: silk is forbidden for men but allowed for women, supported by authentic hadith. However, a small patch of silk on a garment is tolerated. Furthermore, using silk as a pillow or mat is permissible for both genders according to Imam Abu Hanifa, though Imam Malik considers it disliked. This lesson unpacks the evidence and the inter-madhhab difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The text explicitly states that wearing silk (لبس الحرير) is haram for men, halal for women. Note the use of the preposition 'lam' meaning 'upon' (على).
This is the core prohibition.
2
Examine the primary evidence
The hadith from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari explicitly declares gold and silk lawful for females and forbidden for males. This is the main dalil.
Hadith is sahih and has a qati' dalalah.
3
Understand the exception
Another hadith permits a small amount of silk, interpreted as up to four fingers width. This excusable amount is for patches or trimmings, not the whole garment.
The exception is based on the same prophetic tradition.
4
Analyze the pillow ruling
The text states that using silk as a pillow (توسده) or mat (افتراشه) is halal for both men and women according to Imam Abu Hanifa. This is a separate ruling from wearing.
The distinction is based on the direct contact with the body and the nature of use.
5
Contrast with Maliki position
Imam Malik considers using silk as a pillow makruh for men. The Hanafi school relies on the default permissibility (ibaha) in the absence of explicit prohibition.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf with practical implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter on Clothing. Since the author mentioned the preliminaries of the Book of Disliked Matters, he then mentioned what comes to a person from what he needs. So he gave precedence to the chapter on eating and drinking because man's need for eating and drinking is greater than his need for looking, due to the certainty of the first. And in all times, unlike the second. He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'Forbidden for men, not for women, is wearing silk except the extent of four fingers.' Meaning it is forbidden for men, not for women, to wear silk. The letter 'lam' comes in the sense of 'upon' as Allah said: 'And if you do evil, it is upon yourselves' [al-Isra: 7] meaning 'upon them'. The reason silk is forbidden for men but not women is what Abu Musa al-Ash'ari narrated: The Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'Gold and silk are made lawful for the females of my ummah and forbidden for its males.' Related by Ahmad, al-Nasa'i, al-Tirmidhi and he declared it sound. And because it is related from him, peace be upon him, he said: 'Whoever wears silk in this world will not wear it in the Hereafter.' Except that a small amount is excused, and that is the extent of four fingers. Because Ahmad, Muslim, and al-Bukhari related: 'He forbade wearing silk except for the place of two, three, or four fingers.' The hadith. He said: 'And it is permissible to use it as a pillow and a mat.' Meaning for men and women, and this is according to Imam Abu Hanifa. And Malik said: He dislikes that for him. This is from al-Jami' al-Saghir. And he mentioned...

Commentary
This is the beginning of the chapter on dress in the Book of Karahiyah. The author explains why the chapter on eating/drinking came before dress: need for food is constant, while need for clothing and appearance is secondary. The main ruling: wearing pure silk is haram for men, halal for women, based on explicit hadith. However, a small patch up to four fingers width is excused based on the hadith that the Prophet forbade silk except for two, three, or four fingers. Additionally, using silk as a pillow or bedding (tawassud and iftirash) is permissible for both men and women according to Imam Abu Hanifa, but Imam Malik considered it makruh. This is an inter-madhhab difference: Hanafi allows it, Maliki dislikes it.

Key Terms
توسده (tawassuduhu): Using it as a pillow or cushion
افتراشه (iftirashuhu): Using it as a mat or bedding to sit or lie on
حرير (harīr): Silk fabric

Key Takeaway
Wearing pure silk is haram for men except for a small patch ≤4 fingers; using silk as a pillow is permissible per the Hanafi school, though Malik considers it makruh.

Prohibition of Wearing Silk for Men and the Four-Finger Concession
Ruling on wearing pure silk for men and women with dalil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wearing silk for men and women, identify the textual evidence from hadith, and apply the four-finger exception.

Core Idea
Silk is haram for men but halal for women; a small amount up to four fingers is excused.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the foundational rule regarding silk clothing. The Hanafi school distinguishes between men and women based on explicit prophetic hadith. You will also learn the concession for small amounts, which balances strict prohibition with practical ease.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is wearing silk; the ruling is haram for men, halal for women.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith: 'Gold and silk have been made lawful for the females of my ummah and prohibited for its males.'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'harrāma' indicates explicit prohibition for males; 'halāl' indicates permissibility for females.
4
Note the exception
The Prophet allowed up to four fingers based on another hadith: 'He forbade wearing silk except for the place of two or three or four fingers.'
5
Apply the ruling
Any amount more than four fingers is sinful; less is pardoned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لما ذكر مقدمات مسائل الكراهية ذكر ما يتوارد على الإنسان مما يحتاج إليه فقدم فصل الأكل والشرب لأن احتياج الإنسان إلى الأكل والشرب أشد من احتياجه إلى النظر لتحقق الأول. وفي جميع الأوقات دون الثاني ا هـ . قال رحمه الله : (حرم للرجل لا للمرأة لبس الحرير إلا قدر أربع أصابع يعني يحرم على الرجل لا على المرأة لبس الحرير واللام تأتي بمعنى قال الله تعالى وإن أساتم فلها [الإسراء : ] أي فعليها وإنما حرم لبس الحرير على الرجال دون النسء لما روى أبو موسى الأشعري إن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال «أحل الذهب والحرير للإناث من أمتي وحرم على ذكورها رواه أحمد والنسائي والترمذي وصححه. ولما روي عنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال من لبس الحرير في الدنيا لم يلبسه في الآخرة» إلا أن اليسير معفو عنه وهو مقدار أربع أصابع لما روى أحمد ومسلم البخاري نهى عن لبس الحرير إلا موضع أصبعين أو ثلاثة أو أربع الحديث قال رحمه الله : (وحل توسده وافتراشه) يعني للرجال والنساء وهذا عند الإمام. وقال مالك : يكره له ذلك ؛ كذا في الجامع الصغير : وذكر القدوري قول أبي يوسف مع محمد، وذكره أبو الليث مع أبي حنيفة. لمحمد ما روي عن أبي حنيفة أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام نهى عن لبس الحرير والديباج وأن يجلس عليه. رواه البخاري. وقال سعد بن أبي وقاص لئن أتكئ على جمر الغضا أحب إلي من أن اتكئ على مرافق الحرير. وللإمام ما روي أن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام جلس على مرقعة من حرير، ولأن القليل من الملبوس يباح فكذا القليل هنا، ولأن النوم والافتراش والتوسد إهانة ولأن المحرم اللبس والافتراش والنوم علة الجلوس وجعله ستارة وتعليقه وجعله بيتاً ليس عرفاً فلا يحرم ولا يكره تكة الحرير وتكة الديباج ولو جعل الحرير بيتاً أو علقه قال الإمام: لا يكره . وقال محمد: يكره ؛ كذا في المحيط . قال الشراح يعني الرجل والمرأة جميعاً في هذا الحكم يعني في عدم كراهة توسده إلى آخر أو كراهته عند محمد ا هـ . ولك أن تقول تعميم قول أبي يوسف رحمه الله في الكراهة للنساء مشكل فإن قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام حلال لإناثهم» يعم التوسد والافتراش والجلوس والستارة وجعله بيتاً فكيف يتركان العمل بعموم الحديث فليتأمل. وقد يجاب بأن الحل للنساء لأجل التزين للرجال وترغيب الرجل فيها وفي وطئها وتحسينها في منظره فالعلة النقلية منظور فيها إلى هذه العقلية والدليل على ذلك تحريمه على الرجل والحل للنساء والعلة العقلية لم توجد في التوسد وغيره فلهذا قالا يكره ذلك للنساء فتأمل. وفي النصاب ويكره اتخاذ الخلخال في رجل الصغير ا هـ . قال رحمه الله : (ولبس ما سداه حرير ولحمته قطن أو خز) يعني حل للرجال لبس هذا لأن الصحابة الله رضي كانوا يلبسون الخز وهو اسم للمسدي بالحرير، ولأن الثوب يصير ثوباً إلا بالنسج والنسج باللحمة فكانت هي المعتبرة أو تقول لا يكون ثوباً إلا بهما فتكون العلة ذات وجهين فيعتبر التي تظهر في المنظر وهي اللحمة فتكون العبرة لما يظهر دون ما يخفى . والديباج لغة وعرفاً ما كان كله حريراً قال في المغرب : الديباج الذي سداه ولحمته ابريسم: في النهاية وغيرها : وجوه هذه المسألة ثلاثة : الأول ما يكون كله حرير وهو الديباج لا يجوز لبسه في غير الحرب بالاتفاق وأما في الحرب فعند الإمام لا يجوز، وعندهما يجوز . والثاني ما يكون سداه حرير ولحمته غيره ولا بأس به بالحرب وغيره والثالث عكس الثاني وهو مباح في الحرب دون غيره كما سيأتي والخز وبر دابة تخرج من البحر يؤخذ وينسج . قال رحمه الله : ( وعكسه حل في الحرب فقط) يعني ولو عكس المذكور وهو لحمته حريراً وسداه غيره وهو لا يجوز إلا في الحرب لما ذكرنا أن العبرة باللحمة، ولا يجوز لبس الحرير الخالص في الحرب عند الإمام، وعندهما يجوز لما روي أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام رخص في لبس الحرير الخالص في الحرب ورخص في لبس الخز والديباج في الحرب فلأن فيه ضرورة لأن الخالص منه أرفع لعدة السلاح وأهيب في عين العد وليريعه. وللإمام إطلاق
When he mentioned the introductions to the issues of disliked matters, he mentioned what comes to a person that he needs, so he gave precedence to the chapter on eating and drinking because a person's need for eating and drinking is more intense than his need for looking, due to the first being realized at all times unlike the second. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'Prohibited for a man, not for a woman, is wearing silk except to the extent of four fingers.' Meaning, wearing silk is prohibited for a man, not for a woman, and the preposition 'lam' comes in the sense of 'upon', as Allah Almighty said: 'And if you do evil, it is against yourselves' [Al-Isra: 7], i.e., upon them. The prohibition of wearing silk for men without women is based on what Abu Musa Al-Ash'ari narrated that the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Gold and silk have been made lawful for the females of my ummah and prohibited for its males' – narrated by Ahmad, Al-Nasa'i, and Al-Tirmidhi, and he authenticated it. And based on what was narrated from him ﷺ that he said: 'Whoever wears silk in this world will not wear it in the Hereafter.' However, a small amount is pardoned, which is the extent of four fingers, based on what Ahmad and Muslim and Al-Bukhari narrated: 'He forbade wearing silk except for the place of two or three or four fingers.' The hadith. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And it is permissible to use it as a pillow and as bedding.' Meaning for men and women, and this is according to Imam Abu Hanifa. Malik said it is disliked for him; thus in Al-Jami' Al-Saghir. And Al-Quduri mentioned the statement of Abu Yusuf with Muhammad, and Abu Al-Layth mentioned it with Abu Hanifa. According to Muhammad, what is narrated from Abu Hanifa is that the Prophet ﷺ forbade wearing silk and brocade and sitting on it – narrated by Al-Bukhari. And Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas said: 'That I lean on burning embers of al-ghada is more beloved to me than that I lean on silk cushions.' For Imam Abu Hanifa, what is narrated that the Prophet ﷺ sat on a patched piece of silk. And because a small amount of clothing is permissible, so likewise a small amount here. And because sleeping, using as bedding, and using as a pillow are degrading. And because what is prohibited is wearing, while using as bedding, sleeping, and sitting are not [prohibited] in custom, so it is not prohibited nor disliked; the waistband of silk and the waistband of brocade [are not disliked]. And if one makes a tent of silk or hangs it, Imam Abu Hanifa said: it is not disliked. Muhammad said: it is disliked; thus in Al-Muhit. The commentators said: meaning both men and women in this ruling, meaning regarding the absence of dislike for using it as a pillow etc., or its dislike according to Muhammad. And you may say: generalizing the statement of Abu Yusuf regarding dislike for women is problematic, because his statement ﷺ 'lawful for their females' includes using as pillow, bedding, sitting, curtain, and making it a tent, so how can they abandon acting upon the generality of the hadith? So reflect. And it may be answered that the permissibility for women is for the sake of adorning for men and making the man desire her and enjoy her and beautify her in his sight. The transmitted cause is considered in light of this rational cause, and the evidence for that is its prohibition for men and permissibility for women. And this rational cause is not found in using it as a pillow etc., so they both said it is disliked for women. So reflect. In Al-Nisab: and it is disliked to take anklets for the foot of a young boy. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And wearing what has silk warp and cotton or khazz weft.' Meaning it is permissible for men to wear this because the companions, may Allah be pleased with them, used to wear khazz, which is the name for what is woven with silk. And because the garment only becomes a garment through weaving, and weaving is with the weft, so it is the considered part; or you may say that it is not a garment except through both, so the cause has two aspects, so the one that appears in the view is considered, i.e., the weft, so consideration is given to what appears, not what is hidden. Al-Dibaj (brocade) linguistically and customarily is that which is entirely silk. Al-Maghrib says: 'Al-Dibaj is what has both warp and weft of silk.' In Al-Nihayah and others: The aspects of this issue are three: First, what is entirely silk, i.e., al-dibaj, which is not permissible to wear except in war by agreement; as for war, according to Imam Abu Hanifa it is not permissible, while according to the two companions it is permissible. Second, what has silk warp and other weft, and there is no harm in it in war and otherwise. Third, the opposite of the second, which is permissible in war only, as will come. And khazz is the fur of an animal that comes out of the sea, taken and woven. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And its opposite is permissible in war only.' Meaning, if the opposite of what was mentioned occurs, i.e., its weft is silk and its warp is other, then it is not permissible except in war, because, as we mentioned, the consideration is given to the weft. And pure silk is not permissible in war according to Imam Abu Hanifa, while according to the two companions it is permissible, based on what was narrated that the Prophet ﷺ granted concession for wearing pure silk in war and granted concession for wearing khazz and dibaj in war, because there is necessity, since pure silk is more effective for carrying weapons and more awe-inspiring in the eyes of the enemy and more frightening. And according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the hadith is unrestricted.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq concerning the rulings on silk and mixed fabrics. The commentator begins by explaining the order of chapters, noting that the chapter on eating and drinking preceded the chapter on clothing because the need for sustenance is more constant. The main ruling: wearing silk is haram for men but halal for women. The 'lam' in 'lā lil-mar'ah' is interpreted as 'upon', meaning it is not prohibited for women. The evidence is the hadith: 'Gold and silk have been made lawful for the females of my ummah and prohibited for its males.' Also, the Prophet said that wearing silk in this world leads to deprivation in the Hereafter. However, a small amount up to four fingers is excused for men, based on another hadith allowing up to two, three, or four fingers. Using silk as a pillow or bedding is permissible for both men and women according to Imam Abu Hanifa, because the prohibition is specifically on wearing, and using it in other ways is a form of disrespect and thus not prohibited. Imam Malik and others disliked it. The commentator then details an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that using silk as bedding is disliked for women as well, for the rational cause that its permissibility for women is due to adornment for husbands, which does not apply to bedding. The commentator finds this problematic because the hadith's generality would permit all uses for women. The passage also covers mixed fabrics: if the warp is silk and the weft is cotton or khazz, it is permissible for men to wear, because the weft is the visible part and defines the garment. Brocade (dibaj), which is entirely silk, is prohibited except in war. In war, Imam Abu Hanifa maintains the prohibition, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow it due to a concession from the Prophet. The opposite case (silk weft, other warp) is permissible only in war. The commentator ends with the ruling that pure silk is not permissible in war according to Imam Abu Hanifa, but is permissible according to the two companions.

Key Terms
حرير (ḥarīr): Silk fabric; prohibited for men to wear, permissible for women.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the maximum amount of silk a man may wear according to the concession in this lesson?
A) Two fingers
B) Four fingers
C) One handspan
D) No amount is allowed

Key Takeaway
Silk is prohibited for men except for a small amount up to four fingers, based on explicit hadith.

intermediate
8 min
Using Silk as Bedding and Pillow: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on using silk as bedding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the different Hanafi positions on using silk as bedding, identify the proofs from both sides, and evaluate the tarjih.

Core Idea
Imam Abu Hanifa permits silk bedding; the two companions and Malik consider it disliked based on different hadith.

This passage delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the use of silk for purposes other than wearing, such as pillows and bedding. While Imam Abu Hanifa sees it as permissible due to the specificity of the prohibition on wearing, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that it is disliked, especially for women, based on the rational cause of adornment. Understanding this dispute reveals how Hanafi scholars derive rulings from the same texts.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi positions
Imam Abu Hanifa permits using silk as bedding for both men and women. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider it disliked (makruh tanzih) for women, but permissible for men.
2
Identify the proofs for each
Abu Hanifa cites the Prophet sitting on a silk patched piece and the principle that only wearing is prohibited. The Sahibayn cite the hadith forbidding sitting on silk and the statement of Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas.
3
Analyze the underlying rationale
Abu Hanifa: the prohibition is specifically on wearing; other uses are not included. The Sahibayn: the permissibility for women is based on adornment, which is absent in bedding.
4
Evaluate the tarjih
The preponderant view in the Hanafi school is permissibility for men. For women, the Sahibayn's view is strong, but many later scholars follow Abu Hanifa's general permissibility.
5
Apply the ruling
A man may use a silk pillow; a woman may also, but some scholars advise avoidance for women.

لما ذكر مقدمات مسائل الكراهية ذكر ما يتوارد على الإنسان مما يحتاج إليه فقدم فصل الأكل والشرب لأن احتياج الإنسان إلى الأكل والشرب أشد من احتياجه إلى النظر لتحقق الأول. وفي جميع الأوقات دون الثاني ا هـ والسلام قال من لبس الحرير في الدنيا لم يلبسه في الآخرة» إلا أن اليسير معفو عنه وهو مقدار أربع أصابع لما روى أحمد ومسلم البخاري نهى عن لبس الحرير إلا موضع أصبعين أو ثلاثة أو أربع الحديث قال رحمه الله : (وحل توسده وافتراشه) يعني للرجال والنساء وهذا عند الإمام. وقال مالك : يكره له ذلك ؛ كذا في الجامع الصغير : وذكر القدوري قول أبي يوسف مع محمد، وذكره أبو الليث مع أبي حنيفة. لمحمد ما روي عن أبي حنيفة أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام نهى عن لبس الحرير والديباج وأن يجلس عليه. رواه البخاري. وقال سعد بن أبي وقاص لئن أتكئ على جمر الغضا أحب إلي من أن اتكئ على مرافق الحرير. وللإمام ما روي أن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام جلس على مرقعة من حرير، ولأن القليل من الملبوس يباح فكذا القليل هنا، ولأن النوم والافتراش والتوسد إهانة ولأن المحرم اللبس والافتراش والنوم علة الجلوس وجعله ستارة وتعليقه وجعله بيتاً ليس عرفاً فلا يحرم ولا يكره تكة الحرير وتكة الديباج ولو جعل الحرير بيتاً أو علقه قال الإمام: لا يكره . وقال محمد: يكره ؛ كذا في المحيط . قال الشراح يعني الرجل والمرأة جميعاً في هذا الحكم يعني في عدم كراهة توسده إلى آخر أو كراهته عند محمد ا هـ . ولك أن تقول تعميم قول أبي يوسف رحمه الله في الكراهة للنساء مشكل فإن قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام حلال لإناثهم» يعم التوسد والافتراش والجلوس والستارة وجعله بيتاً فكيف Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq concerning the rulings on silk and mixed fabrics. The commentator begins by explaining the order of chapters, noting that the chapter on eating and drinking preceded the chapter on clothing because the need for sustenance is more constant. The main ruling: wearing silk is haram for men but halal for women. The 'lam' in 'lā lil-mar'ah' is interpreted as 'upon', meaning it is not prohibited for women. The evidence is the hadith: 'Gold and silk have been made lawful for the females of my ummah and prohibited for its males.' Also, the Prophet said that wearing silk in this world leads to deprivation in the Hereafter. However, a small amount up to four fingers is excused for men, based on another hadith allowing up to two, three, or four fingers. Using silk as a pillow or bedding is permissible for both men and women according to Imam Abu Hanifa, because the prohibition is specifically on wearing, and using it in other ways is a form of disrespect and thus not prohibited. Imam Malik and others disliked it. The commentator then details an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that using silk as bedding is disliked for women as well, for the rational cause that its permissibility for women is due to adornment for husbands, which does not apply to bedding. The commentator finds this problematic because the hadith's generality would permit all uses for women. The passage also covers mixed fabrics: if the warp is silk and the weft is cotton or khazz, it is permissible for men to wear, because the weft is the visible part and defines the garment. Brocade (dibaj), which is entirely silk, is prohibited except in war. In war, Imam Abu Hanifa maintains the prohibition, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow it due to a concession from the Prophet. The opposite case (silk weft, other warp) is permissible only in war. The commentator ends with the ruling that pure silk is not permissible in war according to Imam Abu Hanifa, but is permissible according to the two companions.

Key Terms
توسد (tawassud): Using something as a pillow.
افتراش (iftirāsh): Using something as bedding.
حرير (ḥarīr): Silk fabric.
ديباج (dībāj): Brocade; a fabric entirely of silk.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, using a silk pillow for a woman is:
A) Permissible
B) Disliked (makruh)
C) Prohibited
D) Obligatory

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits men to use silk as bedding; for women, there is a dispute between permissibility and dislike.

Mixed Fabrics and War Concessions
Rulings on mixed fabrics and war concessions with evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three categories of silk-mixed fabrics and state the Hanafi rulings for each, including the war concession and the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
The weft defines the fabric; pure silk (dibaj) is prohibited except in war where it is permitted by the Sahibayan.

This passage addresses practical questions about fabrics that contain silk partially. The Hanafi school uses a criterion based on the weft (luhmah) to determine the ruling. Additionally, the text discusses the special concession during war, where even pure silk may be worn according to the two companions, due to necessity for awe and effectiveness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three fabric types
1) Pure silk (dibaj: both warp and weft silk). 2) Silk warp, other weft. 3) Other warp, silk weft.
2
Apply the weft rule
The weft (luhmah) determines the ruling. If weft is silk, it is treated as silk; if weft is non-silk, it is permissible.
3
State the ruling for each
1) Dibaj: haram in non-war, disputed in war. 2) Silk warp/other weft: permissible always. 3) Other warp/silk weft: only permissible in war.
4
Examine the war concession
The Sahibayan allow pure silk and dibaj in war; Abu Hanifa does not. The evidence is a hadith of concession.
5
Understand the rationale for war
Necessity for weapon readiness and intimidation of the enemy.

إلى آخر أو كراهته عند محمد ا هـ . ولك أن تقول تعميم قول أبي يوسف رحمه الله في الكراهة للنساء مشكل فإن قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام حلال لإناثهم» يعم التوسد والافتراش والجلوس والستارة وجعله بيتاً فكيف
