للرجال لبس هذا لأن الصحابة الله رضي كانوا يلبسون الخز وهو اسم للمسدي بالحرير، ولأن الثوب يصير ثوباً إلا بالنسج والنسج باللحمة فكانت هي المعتبرة أو تقول لا يكون ثوباً إلا بهما فتكون العلة ذات وجهين فيعتبر التي تظهر في المنظر وهي اللحمة فتكون العبرة لما يظهر دون ما يخفى . والديباج لغة وعرفاً ما كان كله حريراً قال في المغرب : الديباج الذي سداه ولحمته ابريسم: في النهاية وغيرها : وجوه هذه المسألة ثلاثة : الأول ما يكون كله حرير وهو الديباج لا يجوز لبسه في غير الحرب بالاتفاق وأما في الحرب فعند الإمام لا يجوز، وعندهما يجوز . والثاني ما يكون سداه حرير ولحمته غيره ولا بأس به بالحرب وغيره والثالث عكس الثاني وهو مباح في الحرب دون غيره كما سيأتي والخز وبر دابة تخرج من البحر يؤخذ وينسج . قال رحمه الله : ( وعكسه حل في الحرب فقط) يعني ولو عكس المذكور وهو لحمته حريراً وسداه غيره وهو لا يجوز إلا في الحرب لما ذكرنا أن العبرة باللحمة، ولا يجوز لبس الحرير الخالص في الحرب عند الإمام، وعندهما يجوز لما روي أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام رخص في لبس الحرير الخالص في الحرب ورخص في لبس الخز والديباج في الحرب فلأن فيه ضرورة لأن الخالص منه أرفع لعدة السلاح وأهيب في عين العد وليريعه. وللإمام إطلاق
When he mentioned the introductions to the issues of disliked matters, he mentioned what comes to a person that he needs, so he gave precedence to the chapter on eating and drinking because a person's need for eating and drinking is more intense than his need for looking, due to the first being realized at all times unlike the second. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'Prohibited for a man, not for a woman, is wearing silk except to the extent of four fingers.' Meaning, wearing silk is prohibited for a man, not for a woman, and the preposition 'lam' comes in the sense of 'upon', as Allah Almighty said: 'And if you do evil, it is against yourselves' [Al-Isra: 7], i.e., upon them. The prohibition of wearing silk for men without women is based on what Abu Musa Al-Ash'ari narrated that the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Gold and silk have been made lawful for the females of my ummah and prohibited for its males' – narrated by Ahmad, Al-Nasa'i, and Al-Tirmidhi, and he authenticated it. And based on what was narrated from him ﷺ that he said: 'Whoever wears silk in this world will not wear it in the Hereafter.' However, a small amount is pardoned, which is the extent of four fingers, based on what Ahmad and Muslim and Al-Bukhari narrated: 'He forbade wearing silk except for the place of two or three or four fingers.' The hadith. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And it is permissible to use it as a pillow and as bedding.' Meaning for men and women, and this is according to Imam Abu Hanifa. Malik said it is disliked for him; thus in Al-Jami' Al-Saghir. And Al-Quduri mentioned the statement of Abu Yusuf with Muhammad, and Abu Al-Layth mentioned it with Abu Hanifa. According to Muhammad, what is narrated from Abu Hanifa is that the Prophet ﷺ forbade wearing silk and brocade and sitting on it – narrated by Al-Bukhari. And Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas said: 'That I lean on burning embers of al-ghada is more beloved to me than that I lean on silk cushions.' For Imam Abu Hanifa, what is narrated that the Prophet ﷺ sat on a patched piece of silk. And because a small amount of clothing is permissible, so likewise a small amount here. And because sleeping, using as bedding, and using as a pillow are degrading. And because what is prohibited is wearing, while using as bedding, sleeping, and sitting are not [prohibited] in custom, so it is not prohibited nor disliked; the waistband of silk and the waistband of brocade [are not disliked]. And if one makes a tent of silk or hangs it, Imam Abu Hanifa said: it is not disliked. Muhammad said: it is disliked; thus in Al-Muhit. The commentators said: meaning both men and women in this ruling, meaning regarding the absence of dislike for using it as a pillow etc., or its dislike according to Muhammad. And you may say: generalizing the statement of Abu Yusuf regarding dislike for women is problematic, because his statement ﷺ 'lawful for their females' includes using as pillow, bedding, sitting, curtain, and making it a tent, so how can they abandon acting upon the generality of the hadith? So reflect. And it may be answered that the permissibility for women is for the sake of adorning for men and making the man desire her and enjoy her and beautify her in his sight. The transmitted cause is considered in light of this rational cause, and the evidence for that is its prohibition for men and permissibility for women. And this rational cause is not found in using it as a pillow etc., so they both said it is disliked for women. So reflect. In Al-Nisab: and it is disliked to take anklets for the foot of a young boy. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And wearing what has silk warp and cotton or khazz weft.' Meaning it is permissible for men to wear this because the companions, may Allah be pleased with them, used to wear khazz, which is the name for what is woven with silk. And because the garment only becomes a garment through weaving, and weaving is with the weft, so it is the considered part; or you may say that it is not a garment except through both, so the cause has two aspects, so the one that appears in the view is considered, i.e., the weft, so consideration is given to what appears, not what is hidden. Al-Dibaj (brocade) linguistically and customarily is that which is entirely silk. Al-Maghrib says: 'Al-Dibaj is what has both warp and weft of silk.' In Al-Nihayah and others: The aspects of this issue are three: First, what is entirely silk, i.e., al-dibaj, which is not permissible to wear except in war by agreement; as for war, according to Imam Abu Hanifa it is not permissible, while according to the two companions it is permissible. Second, what has silk warp and other weft, and there is no harm in it in war and otherwise. Third, the opposite of the second, which is permissible in war only, as will come. And khazz is the fur of an animal that comes out of the sea, taken and woven. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And its opposite is permissible in war only.' Meaning, if the opposite of what was mentioned occurs, i.e., its weft is silk and its warp is other, then it is not permissible except in war, because, as we mentioned, the consideration is given to the weft. And pure silk is not permissible in war according to Imam Abu Hanifa, while according to the two companions it is permissible, based on what was narrated that the Prophet ﷺ granted concession for wearing pure silk in war and granted concession for wearing khazz and dibaj in war, because there is necessity, since pure silk is more effective for carrying weapons and more awe-inspiring in the eyes of the enemy and more frightening. And according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the hadith is unrestricted.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq concerning the rulings on silk and mixed fabrics. The commentator begins by explaining the order of chapters, noting that the chapter on eating and drinking preceded the chapter on clothing because the need for sustenance is more constant. The main ruling: wearing silk is haram for men but halal for women. The 'lam' in 'lā lil-mar'ah' is interpreted as 'upon', meaning it is not prohibited for women. The evidence is the hadith: 'Gold and silk have been made lawful for the females of my ummah and prohibited for its males.' Also, the Prophet said that wearing silk in this world leads to deprivation in the Hereafter. However, a small amount up to four fingers is excused for men, based on another hadith allowing up to two, three, or four fingers. Using silk as a pillow or bedding is permissible for both men and women according to Imam Abu Hanifa, because the prohibition is specifically on wearing, and using it in other ways is a form of disrespect and thus not prohibited. Imam Malik and others disliked it. The commentator then details an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that using silk as bedding is disliked for women as well, for the rational cause that its permissibility for women is due to adornment for husbands, which does not apply to bedding. The commentator finds this problematic because the hadith's generality would permit all uses for women. The passage also covers mixed fabrics: if the warp is silk and the weft is cotton or khazz, it is permissible for men to wear, because the weft is the visible part and defines the garment. Brocade (dibaj), which is entirely silk, is prohibited except in war. In war, Imam Abu Hanifa maintains the prohibition, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow it due to a concession from the Prophet. The opposite case (silk weft, other warp) is permissible only in war. The commentator ends with the ruling that pure silk is not permissible in war according to Imam Abu Hanifa, but is permissible according to the two companions.

Key Terms
سدى (sadā): Warp (longitudinal threads) of a fabric.
لحمة (luḥmah): Weft (transverse threads) of a fabric.
ديباج (dībāj): Brocade; a fabric entirely of silk (both warp and weft).
خز (khazz): A silk-like fabric made from fur of a sea animal; mixed with silk allowed for men.
حرير (ḥarīr): Silk fabric.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, a fabric with silk warp and cotton weft is:
A) Haram for men
B) Halal for men
C) Only permissible in war
D) Makruh

Key Takeaway
The weft (luhmah) determines the ruling for mixed fabrics; pure silk (dibaj) is haram except in war where it is permitted by the Sahibayn.

Ruling on Remembrance Strings vs. Amulets
Distinguishing permissible remembrance strings from prohibited amulets

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between permissible remembrance strings (ratima) and prohibited amulets (tamima) based on intention and evidence, and state the Hanafi ruling for each.

Core Idea
The string tied for remembrance is mubah; the amulet worn to repel harm is shirk and kufr.

This passage clarifies a common confusion between two similar practices: tying a string on the fingers to remember something, and wearing an amulet for protection. The author traces the pre-Islamic origins and then rules based on intention and prophetic guidance. The key is that the ratima serves a valid purpose and was even commanded by the Prophet ﷺ, while the tamima is rooted in Jahili superstition and amounts to disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two practices
Locate the terms ratima (or ratam) and tamima in the passage. Note that ratima is a string tied for remembrance, while tamima is an amulet worn to repel harm.
The passage also mentions a pre-Islamic custom involving a tree called ratam, but the main focus is the distinction between the two uses of string.
2
Examine the pre-Islamic background
Understand the Jahili practice: strings were tied around the neck or hand to ward off harm. This is the origin of tamima. The ratima, however, is a separate custom for remembrance.
The author mentions both to highlight the difference in intention.
3
Determine the ruling based on intention
The ratima is permissible (mubah) because it serves a valid purpose (remembrance) and the Prophet ﷺ commanded it. The tamima is kufr because it involves superstitious belief in the string's power to repel harm.
The passage explicitly says tamima is kufr according to 'Hudud al-Iman'.
4
Apply the hadith evidence
The Prophet ﷺ said: 'Incantations, amulets, and love charms are shirk.' This hadith directly condemns tamima. The ratima is not included because it is not used for protection but for remembrance.
The hadith is cited as dalil for the prohibition of tamima.
5
Conclude the distinction
Thus, a Muslim may tie a string for memory purposes, but wearing an amulet for protection is forbidden. The deciding factor is the intention and belief attached to the object.
This ruling is based on the Hanafi position that any act resembling shirk is prohibited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرتم هو الرتيمة وهي الخيط للتذكر ليعقد في الأصابع وكذا الرتمة فقيل الرتم ضرب من الشجر. وقال : معناه كان الرجل إذا خرج إلى سفر عمد إلى هذه الشجرة فعقد بعض أغصانها ببعض فإذا وأصابه بتلك الحالة قال لم تخن امرأتي، وإن أصابه قد انحل قال خانتني. ثم الرتيمة قد تشبه بالتميمة على بعض الناس وهو خيط كان يربط في العنق أو في اليد في الجاهلية لدفع المضرة عن أنفسهم. وذكر في حدود الإيمان أنه كفر. والرتيمة مباح لأنها تربط للتذكير عند اليد في الجاهلية لدفع المضرة عن أنفسهم. وذكر في حدود الإيمان أنه كفر . والرتيمة مباح لأنها تربط للتذكير عند النسيان وقد ورد أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام أمر بعض أصحابه بها وتعلق غرض صحيح فلا يكره بخلاف التميمة فإنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال فيها أن الرقي والتمائم والنودة شركة على ما يجيء إن شاء الله تعالى ..
And al-ratam is the ratima, which is a string for remembrance, tied on the fingers, and similarly the ratma; it is said that ratam is a type of tree. And he said: Its meaning is that when a man set out on a journey, he would go to this tree and tie some of its branches together; if he found them in that state, he said 'My wife did not betray me,' and if he found them untied, he said 'She betrayed me.' Then the ratima resembles the tamima for some people; it is a string that was tied around the neck or hand in the Jahiliyyah to repel harm from themselves. And it is mentioned in 'Hudud al-Iman' that it is kufr. And the ratima is mubah because it is tied for remembrance, whereas in the Jahiliyyah it was tied to repel harm from themselves. And it is mentioned in 'Hudud al-Iman' that it is kufr. The ratima is mubah because it is tied for remembrance when forgetting, and it has been reported that the Prophet ﷺ commanded some of his Companions to use it; and it is connected to a valid purpose, so it is not disliked, unlike the tamima, for the Prophet ﷺ said about it: 'Indeed incantations, amulets, and love charms are shirk,' as will come if Allah wills.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a ruling that differentiates between two similar practices: tying a string on the fingers to remember something (ratima) and tying an amulet (tamima) for protection. The author explains the linguistic origins and pre-Islamic practice. The key distinction is that ratima is merely a memory aid, and the Prophet ﷺ even instructed some companions to use it, making it permissible (mubah). Tamima, on the other hand, is a superstitious practice rooted in Jahiliyyah to ward off harm, and it is considered kufr (disbelief) according to the book 'Hudud al-Iman'. The evidence is a hadith stating that incantations, amulets, and love charms are shirk. Therefore, a Muslim may use a string for remembrance as long as no superstitious belief is attached, but wearing amulets for protection is forbidden and a form of shirk.

Key Terms
الرتم (al-ratam): A type of tree, and also refers to the string (ratima) used for remembrance.
الرتيمة (al-ratima): A thread tied on the fingers to remember something; permissible (mubah).
التميمة (al-tamima): An amulet worn to repel harm; considered kufr in Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on using a thread tied on the finger for remembrance (ratima)?
A) Haram
B) Mubah
C) Wajib
D) Makruh

Key Takeaway
The difference between a permissible remembrance string and a forbidden amulet is the intention: remembrance is mubah, protection through the object itself is shirk.

The Permissible Look: Face and Hands of a Non-Mahram Woman
Detailed explanation of the basic ruling with proof and intra-madhhab differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on which body parts of a free non-mahram woman may be looked at and identify the primary evidence from the hadith of Asma bint Abi Bakr.

Core Idea
Only a free woman's face and hands may be looked at without desire; all else is ‘awrah.

In this passage, the commentator of Kanz al-Daqaiq explains the foundational ruling on looking at a non-mahram woman. He addresses a potential ambiguity in the matn's wording and provides the Prophetic evidence that limits permissible looking to the face and hands. The lesson also covers intra-Hanafi differences regarding the foot and forearm, and clarifies that looking through transparent clothes is prohibited when it reveals the figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories of looking
The text begins by listing four types of looking: man-woman, woman-man, man-man, woman-woman. This frames the discussion that follows.
2
Locate the matn ruling
The matn states: 'He does not look at anything other than the face and hands of a free woman.' This is the core ruling.
The matn is from Kanz al-Daqaiq.
3
Analyze the commentator's linguistic refinement
Some thought the matn implied incitement to look at face/hands, but the commentator explains that the preposition 'ila' replaces 'min', making it a prohibition of looking at other parts.
This preserves the intended meaning.
4
Examine the primary evidence: the hadith of Asma
The Prophet ﷺ told Asma that after puberty, only her face and hands may be seen. This hadith is the main dalil for the ruling.
The hadith is ahad (zanni thubut) but its meaning is clear (qati dalala).
5
Note the intra-Hanafi difference on foot and forearm
Abu Hanifa reportedly allowed looking at the foot, and Abu Yusuf at the forearm, but the mufta bihi is that only face and hands are allowed, as there is no necessity for foot/forearm.
The foot is 'awrah for looking but not for salah.
6
Understand the rules on transparent clothing and touching
Looking at a woman's body through clothes is permissible if clothes do not reveal the figure; if they do, it is prohibited. Touching the face is disliked (makruh).
The text distinguishes between looking and touching.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في النظر واللمس
ولما أنهى الكلام على مسائل اللبس وقدمه لشدة الاحتياج إليه ذكر بعده مسائل النظر لأنها أكثر وقوعاً من مسائر الاستبراء فلذا قدمها ومسائل النظر أقسام أربعة : نظر الرجل إلى المرأة، ونظر المرأة إلى الرجل، ونظر الرجل إلى الرجل ونظر المرأة إلى المرأة. والقسم الأول منها على أربعة أقسام نظر الرجل إلى الأجنبية، ونظره إلى زوجته وأمته، ونظره إلى ذوات محارمه، ونظره إلى أمة الغير والدليل على جواز النظر ما روي أن أسماء بنت أبي بكر دخلت على رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وعليها ثياب رقاق فأعرض عنها رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وقال : يا أسماء إن المرأة إذا بلغت المحيض لم يصلح أن يرى منها إلا هذا وهذا أو أشار إلى وجهه وكفيه. قال رحمه الله : (لا ينظر إلى غير وجه الحرة وكفيها قال الشارح وهذا الكلام فيه خلل لأنه يؤدي إلى أنه لا ينظر إلى شيء من الأشياء إلا إلى وجه الحرة وكفيها فيكون تحريضاً إلى النظر إلى هذين العضوين وإلى ترك النظر إلى كل شيء سواهما .اهـ. ولا يخفى على متأمل عدم هذا الخلل لأن حرف «إلى» بدل عن (من)) الابتدائية التي إلى غايتها فهو في قوة المنطوق، فالتقدير لا يجوز له النظر من المرأة إلى غير الوجه وكفيها فقد أفاد منع النظر منها غير الوجه وكفيها إلا التحريض فتدبره واستدل الشارح على جواز النظر إلى ما ذكر بقوله تعالى ولا يبدين زينتهن إلا ما ظهر منها [النور: ۳۱] قال علي وابن عباس : ما ظهر منها الكحل والخاتم لا الوجه كله والكف فلا يفيد المدعي فتأمل والأصل في هذا أن المرأة عورة مستورة لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «المرأة عورة مستورة إلا ما استثناه الشرع وهما عضوان، ولأن المرأة لا بدلها من للمعاملة مع الأجانب فلا بد لها من إبداء الوجه لتعرف فتطالب بالثمن ويرد عليها بالعيب، ولا بد من إبداء الكف للأخذ والعطاء، وهذا يفيد أن القدم لا يجوز النظر إليه، وعن الإمام أنه يجوز ولا ضرورة في إبداء القدم فهو عورة في حق النظر وليس بعورة في حق الصلاة؛ كذا في المحيط . وعن الثاني يجوز النظر إلى ذراعيها أيضاً لأنه يبدو منها عادة، وما عدا هذه الأعضاء لا يجوز النظر إليها لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «من نظر إلى محاسن امرأة أجنبية عن شهوة صب في عينيه الآنك يوم القيامة». الحديث. وهو الرصاص المذاب. وقالوا : ولا بأس بالتأمل في جسدها وعليها ثياب ما لم يكن ثوب بيان حجمها فلا ينظر إليه حينئذ لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام" من تأمل خلف امرأة من ورائها ثيابها حتى تبين له عظامها لم يرح رائحة الجنة وإذا كان الثوب لا يصف عظامها فالنظر لى الثوب دون عظامها فصار كما لو نظر إلى خيمة فيها فلا بأس به قيدنا بالنظر لأنه يكره له أن يمس الوجه من الأجنبية ؛ كذا في قاضيخان. وشمل كلامه الحر المسلم البالغ والرقيق البالغ والصبي المراهق والكافر؛ كذا في الغياثية وفيها ولا بأس بالنظر إلى شعر الكافرة. اهـ.
والكف
حجم
قال رحمه الله : ولا ينظر من اشتهى إلى وجهها إلا الحاكم والشاهد وينظر الطبيب إلى موضع مرضها والأصل أنه لا يجوز أن ينظر إلى وجه الأجنبية بشهوة لما روينا إلا للضرورة إذا تيقن بالشهوة أو شك فيها، وفي نظر من ذكرنا مع الشهوة ضرورة فيجوز، وكذا نظر الحاقن والحاقنة فيجوز وكذا نظر الخاتن إذا أراد أن يداوي مع الختان، وكذا يجوز النظر للهزال الفاحش لأنه امارة البرص ويجب على القاضي والشاهد أن يقصد أداء الشهادة والحكم لإقضاء الشهوة تحرزاً عن القبح بقدر الأمكان هذا وقت الأداء، وأما وقت التحمل فلا يجوز أن ينظر إليها مع الشهوة لأنه يوجد غيره مما لا يشتهي فلا حاجة إليه. قال في الغياثية واختلف المشايخ فيما إذا ادعي إلى التحمل وهو يعلم أنه إذا نظر إليها يشتهي، فمنهم من جوز ذلك بشرط أن يقصد تحمل الشهادة لاقضاء الشهوة، والأصح أنه لا يجوز له ذلك. قال بعض شراح الهداية : وقد تنور هذا إباحه النظر إلى العورة الغليظة عند الزنا لإقامة الشهادة عليه ولا يقال الشاهد مخير هنا بين حسنتين إقامة الحد والتحرز عن التملك وهو أفضل، فإذا كان أفضل فكيف جاز النظر لأقامة الشهادة لأنا نقول : الضرورة والحاجة محققة في النظر إلى العورة الغليظة عند التحمل بالنسبة لإرادة إقامة الحد وإن لم تكن الضرورة والحاجة محققة بالنظر إلى الستر فالإباحة بالنظر إلى الأول فإن قلت : لماذا جاز لشاهد الزنا النظر عند التحمل ولو اشتهى ولم يجز لغيره وقت التحمل؟ قلنا : إنما جاز له لأن مقصوده إقامة الشهادة فلهذه الضرورة جاز قالوا لأنه يوجد غيره ممن لا يشتهي . فإن قيل : يمكن هنا أيضاً أن يوجد غيره ممن لا يشتهي قلنا : لو طلب غيره ممن لا يشتهي لفرغ من فعل الزنا فلهذا اجاز هنا ولو اشتهى فتدبره والطبيب إنما يجوز له ذلك إذا لم يوجد امرأة طبيبة فلو
Chapter on Looking and Touching. When he finished the discussion on matters of clothing and presented it due to the severe need, he mentioned after it the matters of looking because they occur more frequently than matters of istibra', so he presented them. And the matters of looking are four categories: a man looking at a woman, a woman looking at a man, a man looking at a man, and a woman looking at a woman. The first division has four subcategories: a man looking at a non-mahram woman, his looking at his wife and his slave woman, his looking at his mahrams, and his looking at another's slave woman. The evidence for the permissibility of looking is what is narrated that Asma bint Abi Bakr entered upon the Messenger of Allah ﷺ wearing thin clothes, so the Messenger of Allah ﷺ turned away from her and said: 'O Asma, indeed when a woman reaches menstruation, nothing should be seen of her except this and this' — and he pointed to his face and hands. He (the author) said: 'He does not look at anything other than the face and hands of a free woman.' The commentator said: 'This statement has a flaw because it implies that he should not look at any thing except the face and hands of a free woman, thus it would be incitement to look at these two parts and to refrain from looking at everything else.' End quote. But it is not hidden from one who reflects that there is no such flaw, because the letter 'ila' replaces the preposition 'min' which denotes the starting point, so it is effectively explicit: the estimation is 'he is not permitted to look from the woman at other than the face and hands,' so it conveys the prohibition of looking at other than the face and hands, not incitement. So reflect on this. And the commentator cited as evidence for the permissibility of looking at what was mentioned the saying of Allah: '...and not display their adornment except what appears thereof' (An-Nur 24:31). Ali and Ibn Abbas said: 'What appears [is] kohl and the ring, not the entire face and hand.' So it does not benefit the claimant — reflect. And the basis in this is that the woman is 'awrah, covered, due to his ﷺ saying: 'The woman is 'awrah, covered, except what the law excepted, which are two parts.' And because a woman cannot avoid dealing with strangers, she must show her face to be recognized, so that she may be demanded the price and returned due to defect, and she must show the hand for taking and giving. This indicates that the foot is not permissible to look at. And from Imam Abu Hanifa it is reported that it is permissible, but there is no necessity to show the foot, so it is 'awrah with respect to looking but not 'awrah with respect to prayer; thus in al-Muhit. And from the second (Abu Yusuf) it is permissible to look at her forearms as well because they usually appear. Other than these parts, it is not permissible to look at them due to his ﷺ saying: 'Whoever looks at the beauties of a non-mahram woman with desire, on the Day of Judgment molten lead will be poured into his eyes.' The hadith. It is molten lead. And they said: There is no harm in contemplating her body while she is clothed, as long as the clothes do not reveal her figure; then he should not look at that time, due to his ﷺ saying: 'Whoever contemplates a woman from behind her clothes until her bones become apparent to him will not smell the fragrance of Paradise.' If the clothes do not describe her bones, then looking at the clothes without her bones is like looking at a tent that contains her — there is no harm. We restricted it to looking, because it is disliked for him to touch the face of a non-mahram woman; thus in Qadikhan. And his statement includes the free Muslim adult, the slave adult, the adolescent boy, and the disbeliever; thus in al-Ghiyathiyya, and in it: 'There is no harm in looking at the hair of a disbelieving woman.' End. And the hand volume. He said: 'And he who desires must not look at her face except the judge and the witness; and the doctor may look at the site of her illness.' The principle is that it is not permissible to look at the face of a non-mahram woman with desire due to what we narrated, except for necessity when desire is certain or doubted. In the case of those we mentioned, looking with desire is a necessity, so it is permissible. Similarly, the male and female cupper may look, and the circumciser may look if he wants to treat during circumcision. Similarly, looking is permissible if there is extreme thinness (emaciation) because it is a sign of leprosy. And it is obligatory for the judge and the witness to intend the fulfillment of testimony and judgment in order to repel desire, to avoid ugliness as much as possible. This is at the time of performance (ada'), but at the time of bearing witness (tahammul), it is not permissible to look at her with desire because there exists another who does not desire, so there is no need. Al-Ghiyathiyya said: The scholars differed concerning one who is called to bear witness and knows that if he looks at her he will desire. Some permitted that on condition that he intends to bear testimony in order to repel desire; the more correct view is that it is not permissible for him. Some commentators of al-Hidayah said: This has clarified the permissibility of looking at the thick 'awrah during zina to establish testimony against him. It cannot be said that the witness is here given a choice between two good acts: establishing the hadd and avoiding looking at the 'awrah, and the latter is better. If it is better, then how is looking permitted to establish testimony? We say: Necessity and need are actualized in looking at the thick 'awrah during bearing witness for the sake of establishing the hadd, even if necessity and need are not actualized in looking at the covering. So permissibility is based on the first. If you say: Why is it permitted for the witness of zina to look during bearing witness even if he desires, but not for others during bearing witness? We say: It is only permitted for him because his purpose is to establish testimony; due to this necessity it is permitted. They said: Because there exists another who does not desire. If it is said: Here too there could be another who does not desire, we say: If he sought another who does not desire, the act of zina would be completed. Therefore, it is permitted here even if he desires. So reflect. And the doctor is only permitted that if there is no female doctor; if there is...
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the ruling of looking at non-mahram women. The author first categorizes looking into four types, then focuses on a man looking at a non-mahram woman. The matn states that a man may not look at anything other than the face and hands of a free woman. The commentator addresses a potential linguistic flaw in the matn, arguing that the wording actually prohibits looking at other parts rather than inciting looking at the face and hands. Evidence is provided from the hadith of Asma and the Quranic verse, though the interpretation of Ali and Ibn Abbas is noted as not fully supporting the ruling. The general principle is that the woman is 'awrah except what is exempted by law: the face and hands, due to necessity for trade and testimony. There is an ikhtilaf on whether the foot and forearm are included: the preferred view is that the foot is 'awrah for looking (not for salah), and the forearm is also 'awrah. Looking at the body through transparent clothes is prohibited if it reveals the figure, otherwise permissible. Touching the face is disliked. The text then discusses exceptions: a judge, witness, doctor, cupper, and circumciser may look even with desire if necessary, but only at the time of giving testimony (ada'), not at bearing witness (tahammul) unless there is no alternative. For zina testimony, looking at the private parts is permitted to establish the hadd, with a discussion of the hierarchy of necessities. Finally, the doctor may look only if no female doctor is available.

Key Terms
الأجنبية (al-ajnabiyya): A woman with whom marriage is not prohibited by blood, fosterage, or marriage, and who is not one's wife or slave.
العورة (al-‘awra): Parts of the body that must be covered; for a free woman, all except the face and hands in the context of looking.
الشهوة (al-shahwa): Sexual desire; looking with desire is prohibited except under necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition for looking at the face of a free non-mahram woman to be permissible?
A) It is always permissible regardless of desire.
B) It is permissible only if she is a mahram.
C) It is permissible only if the look is without sexual desire, and it is confined to the face and hands.
D) It is permissible only if she is wearing a headscarf.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows a man to look at the face and hands of a free non-mahram woman without desire; all other parts are 'awrah and prohibited to look at.

intermediate
10 min
Necessity Exceptions: Judge, Witness, Doctor, and Similar Necessities
Explanation of exceptional permissions for looking with desire under necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi exceptions that permit looking at a non-mahram woman with desire under necessity, specifically for a judge, witness, doctor, cupper, and circumciser, and distinguish between ada' and tahammul.

Core Idea
Necessity (darura) permits looking with desire for specific legal and medical purposes.

This passage continues the discussion by addressing exceptions to the general prohibition of looking at a non-mahram woman with desire. It covers the judge and witness during testimony, the doctor treating illness, and other professionals like cuppers and circumcisers. The key distinction is between the time of bearing witness (tahammul) and the time of giving testimony (ada'), and the requirement that no alternative exists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle for looking with desire
The initial rule states: 'He who desires must not look at her face except the judge and the witness.' The baseline is prohibition unless necessity overrides.
2
List the exceptional categories
The text explicitly mentions: judge, witness, doctor, male and female cupper, circumciser, and cases of extreme thinness (sign of leprosy).
3
Understand the distinction between ada' and tahammul for witness
At the time of giving testimony (ada'), looking with desire is permissible due to necessity. But at the time of bearing witness (tahammul), it is not allowed because there is no immediate necessity and another witness who does not desire may be found.
This distinction is crucial.
4
Analyze the ruling for the doctor
The doctor may look at the site of illness even with desire only if no female doctor is available. If a female doctor is available, she must be consulted first.
The condition of necessity is strict.
5
Consider the case of zina testimony
For establishing the hadd for zina, looking at the 'awrah (private parts) is permitted at the time of bearing witness because of the greater necessity to uphold the hadd. The text addresses objections that there might be another witness who does not desire.
This is an exceptional allowance.

قال رحمه الله : ولا ينظر من اشتهى إلى وجهها إلا الحاكم والشاهد وينظر الطبيب إلى موضع مرضها والأصل أنه لا يجوز أن ينظر إلى وجه الأجنبية بشهوة لما روينا إلا للضرورة إذا تيقن بالشهوة أو شك فيها، وفي نظر من ذكرنا مع الشهوة ضرورة فيجوز، وكذا نظر الحاقن والحاقنة فيجوز وكذا نظر الخاتن إذا أراد أن يداوي مع الختان، وكذا يجوز النظر للهزال الفاحش لأنه امارة البرص ويجب على القاضي والشاهد أن يقصد أداء الشهادة والحكم لإقضاء الشهوة تحرزاً عن القبح بقدر الأمكان هذا وقت الأداء، وأما وقت التحمل فلا يجوز أن ينظر إليها مع الشهوة لأنه يوجد غيره مما لا يشتهي فلا حاجة إليه. قال في الغياثية واختلف المشايخ فيما إذا ادعي إلى التحمل وهو يعلم أنه إذا نظر إليها يشتهي، فمنهم من جوز ذلك بشرط أن يقصد تحمل الشهادة لاقضاء الشهوة، والأصح أنه لا يجوز له ذلك. قال بعض شراح الهداية : وقد تنور هذا إباحه النظر إلى العورة الغليظة عند الزنا لإقامة الشهادة عليه ولا يقال الشاهد مخير هنا بين حسنتين إقامة الحد والتحرز عن التملك وهو أفضل، فإذا كان أفضل فكيف جاز النظر لأقامة الشهادة لأنا نقول : الضرورة والحاجة محققة في النظر إلى العورة الغليظة عند التحمل بالنسبة لإرادة إقامة الحد وإن لم تكن الضرورة والحاجة محققة بالنظر إلى الستر فالإباحة بالنظر إلى الأول فإن قلت : لماذا جاز لشاهد الزنا النظر عند التحمل ولو اشتهى ولم يجز لغيره وقت التحمل؟ قلنا : إنما جاز له لأن مقصوده إقامة الشهادة فلهذه الضرورة جاز قالوا لأنه يوجد غيره ممن لا يشتهي . فإن قيل : يمكن هنا أيضاً أن يوجد غيره ممن لا يشتهي قلنا : لو طلب غيره ممن لا يشتهي لفرغ من فعل الزنا فلهذا اجاز هنا ولو اشتهى فتدبره والطبيب إنما يجوز له ذلك إذا لم يوجد امرأة طبيبة فلو
Chapter on Looking and Touching. When he finished the discussion on matters of clothing and presented it due to the severe need, he mentioned after it the matters of looking because they occur more frequently than matters of istibra', so he presented them. And the matters of looking are four categories: a man looking at a woman, a woman looking at a man, a man looking at a man, and a woman looking at a woman. The first division has four subcategories: a man looking at a non-mahram woman, his looking at his wife and his slave woman, his looking at his mahrams, and his looking at another's slave woman. The evidence for the permissibility of looking is what is narrated that Asma bint Abi Bakr entered upon the Messenger of Allah ﷺ wearing thin clothes, so the Messenger of Allah ﷺ turned away from her and said: 'O Asma, indeed when a woman reaches menstruation, nothing should be seen of her except this and this' — and he pointed to his face and hands. He (the author) said: 'He does not look at anything other than the face and hands of a free woman.' The commentator said: 'This statement has a flaw because it implies that he should not look at any thing except the face and hands of a free woman, thus it would be incitement to look at these two parts and to refrain from looking at everything else.' End quote. But it is not hidden from one who reflects that there is no such flaw, because the letter 'ila' replaces the preposition 'min' which denotes the starting point, so it is effectively explicit: the estimation is 'he is not permitted to look from the woman at other than the face and hands,' so it conveys the prohibition of looking at other than the face and hands, not incitement. So reflect on this. And the commentator cited as evidence for the permissibility of looking at what was mentioned the saying of Allah: '...and not display their adornment except what appears thereof' (An-Nur 24:31). Ali and Ibn Abbas said: 'What appears [is] kohl and the ring, not the entire face and hand.' So it does not benefit the claimant — reflect. And the basis in this is that the woman is 'awrah, covered, due to his ﷺ saying: 'The woman is 'awrah, covered, except what the law excepted, which are two parts.' And because a woman cannot avoid dealing with strangers, she must show her face to be recognized, so that she may be demanded the price and returned due to defect, and she must show the hand for taking and giving. This indicates that the foot is not permissible to look at. And from Imam Abu Hanifa it is reported that it is permissible, but there is no necessity to show the foot, so it is 'awrah with respect to looking but not 'awrah with respect to prayer; thus in al-Muhit. And from the second (Abu Yusuf) it is permissible to look at her forearms as well because they usually appear. Other than these parts, it is not permissible to look at them due to his ﷺ saying: 'Whoever looks at the beauties of a non-mahram woman with desire, on the Day of Judgment molten lead will be poured into his eyes.' The hadith. It is molten lead. And they said: There is no harm in contemplating her body while she is clothed, as long as the clothes do not reveal her figure; then he should not look at that time, due to his ﷺ saying: 'Whoever contemplates a woman from behind her clothes until her bones become apparent to him will not smell the fragrance of Paradise.' If the clothes do not describe her bones, then looking at the clothes without her bones is like looking at a tent that contains her — there is no harm. We restricted it to looking, because it is disliked for him to touch the face of a non-mahram woman; thus in Qadikhan. And his statement includes the free Muslim adult, the slave adult, the adolescent boy, and the disbeliever; thus in al-Ghiyathiyya, and in it: 'There is no harm in looking at the hair of a disbelieving woman.' End. And the hand volume. He said: 'And he who desires must not look at her face except the judge and the witness; and the doctor may look at the site of her illness.' The principle is that it is not permissible to look at the face of a non-mahram woman with desire due to what we narrated, except for necessity when desire is certain or doubted. In the case of those we mentioned, looking with desire is a necessity, so it is permissible. Similarly, the male and female cupper may look, and the circumciser may look if he wants to treat during circumcision. Similarly, looking is permissible if there is extreme thinness (emaciation) because it is a sign of leprosy. And it is obligatory for the judge and the witness to intend the fulfillment of testimony and judgment in order to repel desire, to avoid ugliness as much as possible. This is at the time of performance (ada'), but at the time of bearing witness (tahammul), it is not permissible to look at her with desire because there exists another who does not desire, so there is no need. Al-Ghiyathiyya said: The scholars differed concerning one who is called to bear witness and knows that if he looks at her he will desire. Some permitted that on condition that he intends to bear testimony in order to repel desire; the more correct view is that it is not permissible for him. Some commentators of al-Hidayah said: This has clarified the permissibility of looking at the thick 'awrah during zina to establish testimony against him. It cannot be said that the witness is here given a choice between two good acts: establishing the hadd and avoiding looking at the 'awrah, and the latter is better. If it is better, then how is looking permitted to establish testimony? We say: Necessity and need are actualized in looking at the thick 'awrah during bearing witness for the sake of establishing the hadd, even if necessity and need are not actualized in looking at the covering. So permissibility is based on the first. If you say: Why is it permitted for the witness of zina to look during bearing witness even if he desires, but not for others during bearing witness? We say: It is only permitted for him because his purpose is to establish testimony; due to this necessity it is permitted. They said: Because there exists another who does not desire. If it is said: Here too there could be another who does not desire, we say: If he sought another who does not desire, the act of zina would be completed. Therefore, it is permitted here even if he desires. So reflect. And the doctor is only permitted that if there is no female doctor; if there is...
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the ruling of looking at non-mahram women. The author first categorizes looking into four types, then focuses on a man looking at a non-mahram woman. The matn states that a man may not look at anything other than the face and hands of a free woman. The commentator addresses a potential linguistic flaw in the matn, arguing that the wording actually prohibits looking at other parts rather than inciting looking at the face and hands. Evidence is provided from the hadith of Asma and the Quranic verse, though the interpretation of Ali and Ibn Abbas is noted as not fully supporting the ruling. The general principle is that the woman is 'awrah except what is exempted by law: the face and hands, due to necessity for trade and testimony. There is an ikhtilaf on whether the foot and forearm are included: the preferred view is that the foot is 'awrah for looking (not for salah), and the forearm is also 'awrah. Looking at the body through transparent clothes is prohibited if it reveals the figure, otherwise permissible. Touching the face is disliked. The text then discusses exceptions: a judge, witness, doctor, cupper, and circumciser may look even with desire if necessary, but only at the time of giving testimony (ada'), not at bearing witness (tahammul) unless there is no alternative. For zina testimony, looking at the private parts is permitted to establish the hadd, with a discussion of the hierarchy of necessities. Finally, the doctor may look only if no female doctor is available.

Key Terms
الحاكم (al-hakim): The judge who passes judgment; allowed to look at the face of a non-mahram woman during testimony even if desire arises.
الطبيب (al-tabib): Doctor; allowed to look at the site of illness of a non-mahram woman if no female doctor is available, even with desire.
الشهادة (al-shahada): Testimony; the witness may look at the face of a non-mahram woman with desire at the time of giving testimony (ada') but not at the time of bearing witness (tahammul) if another non-desiring witness exists.
التحمل (al-tahammul): The act of bearing witness (receiving knowledge of the event); it is a stage before giving testimony (ada').
الحاقن (al-haqin): A cupper (male) who performs hijama; also allowed to look at the body of a non-mahram woman with desire for treatment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition may a witness look at the face of a non-mahram woman with desire?
A) At any time during the judicial process.
B) Only at the time of bearing witness (tahammul).
C) Only at the time of giving testimony (ada'), and he must intend to fulfill his duty.
D) Only if she is a disbeliever.

Key Takeaway
Necessity permits looking with desire for specific legal and medical purposes, but only when no alternative exists and the purpose is purely for the necessity.

Looking at Non-Mahram Women: Prohibitions and Exceptions
Rulings on looking at non-mahram women with exceptions for necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general ruling on looking at non-mahram women, explain the exceptions for necessity, and identify which body parts are considered 'awrah for looking according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Looking with desire at a non-mahram woman is haram, but necessity permits physicians, judges, and witnesses to look; even the genitals may be seen to establish a hadd penalty.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on looking at non-mahram women, a topic that balances the prohibition of desire-driven gazes with the demands of necessity. Through the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, we explore which body parts are off-limits, when looking is permissible despite desire, and the important intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a witness who anticipates desire may look.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
Determine that looking at a non-mahram woman with sexual desire (shahwah) is haram. This is the baseline from which exceptions are carved.
The hadith threatening molten lead is the primary evidence.
2
Examine the proof (dalil)
Locate the hadith cited: 'Whoever looks at the beauties of a non-mahram woman with desire...' and understand how it proves prohibition (wajh al-dalalah).
The threat of punishment indicates haram.
3
List the exceptions for necessity
Identify the categories where looking is permitted even with desire: physician, judge, witness, circumciser, and cases of extreme emaciation. Note that the permission is conditional on genuine need.
The principle is that necessity overrides the prohibition.
4
Determine the 'awrah for looking
Understand which body parts are considered 'awrah for the purpose of looking: face and hands are permissible without desire; forearms are disputed (Abu Yusuf permits); foot is 'awrah for looking but not for prayer.
The ruling differs from 'awrah for prayer.
5
Analyze the witness dispute
Examine the intra-Hanafi difference on whether a witness who knows he will desire may look when summoned to bear testimony. The correct view (al-ashahh) is that he should not if another person without desire is available.
An exception is made for witnessing adultery to establish the hadd penalty.

وينظر الطبيب إلى موضع مرضها وينظر الرجل إلى الرجل إلا العورة والمرأة للمرأة بالعيب، ولا بد من إبداء الكف للأخذ والعطاء، وهذا يفيد أن القدم لا يجوز النظر إليه، وعن الإمام أنه يجوز ولا ضرورة في إبداء القدم فهو عورة في حق النظر وليس بعورة في حق الصلاة؛ كذا في المحيط . وعن الثاني يجوز النظر إلى ذراعيها أيضاً لأنه يبدو منها عادة، وما عدا هذه الأعضاء لا يجوز النظر إليها لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «من نظر إلى محاسن امرأة أجنبية عن شهوة صب في عينيه الآنك يوم القيامة». الحديث. وهو الرصاص المذاب. وقالوا : ولا بأس بالتأمل في جسدها وعليها ثياب ما لم يكن ثوب بيان حجمها فلا ينظر إليه حينئذ لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام" من تأمل خلف امرأة من ورائها ثيابها حتى تبين له عظامها لم يرح رائحة الجنة وإذا كان الثوب لا يصف عظامها فالنظر لى الثوب دون عظامها فصار كما لو نظر إلى خيمة فيها فلا بأس به قيدنا بالنظر لأنه يكره له أن يمس الوجه من الأجنبية ؛ كذا في قاضيخان. وشمل كلامه الحر المسلم البالغ والرقيق البالغ والصبي المراهق والكافر؛ كذا في الغياثية وفيها ولا بأس بالنظر إلى شعر الكافرة. اهـ.

قال رحمه الله : ولا ينظر من اشتهى إلى وجهها إلا الحاكم والشاهد وينظر الطبيب إلى موضع مرضها والأصل أنه لا يجوز أن ينظر إلى وجه الأجنبية بشهوة لما روينا إلا للضرورة إذا تيقن بالشهوة أو شك فيها، وفي نظر من ذكرنا مع الشهوة ضرورة فيجوز، وكذا نظر الحاقن والحاقنة فيجوز وكذا نظر الخاتن إذا أراد أن يداوي مع الختان، وكذا يجوز النظر للهزال الفاحش لأنه امارة البرص ويجب على القاضي والشاهد أن يقصد أداء الشهادة والحكم لإقضاء الشهوة تحرزاً عن القبح بقدر الأمكان هذا وقت الأداء، وأما وقت التحمل فلا يجوز أن ينظر إليها مع الشهوة لأنه يوجد غيره مما لا يشتهي فلا حاجة إليه. قال في الغياثية واختلف المشايخ فيما إذا ادعي إلى التحمل وهو يعلم أنه إذا نظر إليها يشتهي، فمنهم من جوز ذلك بشرط أن يقصد تحمل الشهادة لاقضاء الشهوة، والأصح أنه لا يجوز له ذلك. قال بعض شراح الهداية : وقد تنور هذا إباحه النظر إلى العورة الغليظة عند الزنا لإقامة الشهادة عليه ولا يقال الشاهد مخير هنا بين حسنتين إقامة الحد والتحرز عن التملك وهو أفضل، فإذا كان أفضل فكيف جاز النظر لأقامة الشهادة لأنا نقول : الضرورة والحاجة محققة في النظر إلى العورة الغليظة عند التحمل بالنسبة لإرادة إقامة الحد وإن لم تكن الضرورة والحاجة محققة بالنظر إلى الستر فالإباحة بالنظر إلى الأول فإن قلت : لماذا جاز لشاهد الزنا النظر عند التحمل ولو اشتهى ولم يجز لغيره وقت التحمل؟ قلنا : إنما جاز له لأن مقصوده إقامة الشهادة فلهذه الضرورة جاز قالوا لأنه يوجد غيره ممن لا يشتهي . فإن قيل : يمكن هنا أيضاً أن يوجد غيره ممن لا يشتهي قلنا : لو طلب غيره ممن لا يشتهي لفرغ من فعل الزنا فلهذا اجاز هنا ولو اشتهى فتدبره
The physician looks at the place of her illness, and a man looks at a man except the 'awrah, and a woman looks at a woman with a defect. It is necessary to show the palm for taking and giving. This indicates that the foot is not permissible to look at. From Imam [Abu Hanifa?] that it is permissible, but there is no necessity to show the foot, so it is 'awrah with respect to looking but not 'awrah with respect to prayer; thus in al-Muhit. And from the second [Abu Yusuf?] it is permissible to look at her forearms as well because it normally appears from her. Apart from these body parts, it is not permissible to look at them due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever looks at the beauties of a non-mahram woman with desire, molten lead will be poured into his eyes on the Day of Resurrection.' The hadith. And it is molten lead. And they said: There is no harm in contemplating her body while she is wearing clothes, as long as the clothes do not show her size; so he should not look at her at that time, due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever contemplates a woman from behind her clothes until her bones become apparent to him will not smell the fragrance of Paradise.' And if the clothes do not show her bones, then looking at the clothes without her bones becomes like looking at a tent containing her; there is no harm in it. We restricted it to looking because it is disliked for him to touch the face of a non-mahram woman; thus in Qadikhan. And his speech includes the free Muslim adult, the adult slave, the adolescent boy, and the disbeliever; thus in al-Ghiyathiyyah, and in it: There is no harm in looking at the hair of a disbelieving woman. End.

He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'And one who desires should not look at her face except the judge and the witness, and the physician looks at the place of her illness.' The principle is that it is not permissible to look at the face of a non-mahram woman with desire due to what we narrated, except for necessity if he is certain of desire or doubts it. And in the case of those we mentioned, looking with desire is a necessity, so it is permissible. Similarly, looking by the one who performs circumcision (khatan) is permissible, and the one who performs circumcision for a girl (khatinah) is permissible. Similarly, looking by the circumciser (khatin) when he wants to treat with circumcision. Similarly, looking at extreme emaciation is permissible because it is a sign of leprosy. And the judge and witness must intend to perform testimony and judgment to fulfill the need for it, avoiding the ugly as much as possible. This is at the time of performance. As for the time of bearing (testimony), it is not permissible for him to look at her with desire because there is someone else who does not desire, so there is no need. Al-Ghiyathiyyah said: The scholars differed regarding one who is called to bear testimony while knowing that if he looks at her, he will desire. Some permitted it on condition that he intends to bear testimony to fulfill the need for it, but the correct view is that it is not permissible for him. Some commentators of al-Hidayah said: This has illuminated the permissibility of looking at the thick 'awrah during adultery to establish testimony against him. And it is not said that the witness is given a choice between two good deeds: establishing the hadd and avoiding looking, which is better. If it is better, how is looking to establish testimony permissible? We say: Need and necessity are realized in looking at the thick 'awrah when bearing testimony in order to establish the hadd, even if need and necessity are not realized in looking at the covering. So permissibility is due to the first. If you say: Why is it permissible for the witness of adultery to look at the time of bearing even if he desires, but not for others at the time of bearing? We say: It is only permissible for him because his purpose is to establish testimony; due to this necessity it is permissible. They said: Because there is someone else who does not desire. If it is said: Here too someone else who does not desire can be found. We say: If he sought someone else who does not desire, the act of adultery would have been completed, so it is permitted here even if he desires. So ponder.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It addresses the general prohibition of looking at non-mahram women and the exceptions where it becomes permissible. The author begins by stating that a physician may look at the place of illness, establishing a principle of necessity. The normal rule is that a man may only look at another man's body except the 'awrah (navel to knee), and a woman at a woman's body except the 'awrah. However, for a non-mahram woman, the default is greater restriction. The text notes that the hands (palms) must be shown for transactions, indicating they are not 'awrah for that purpose. There is a difference of opinion: Imam Abu Hanifa is reported to allow looking at the foot, but since there is no necessity, it remains 'awrah for looking. Abu Yusuf allows looking at the forearms because they are commonly exposed. All other body parts are forbidden to look at, supported by the hadith that threatening molten lead for looking with desire. The passage then discusses looking at a woman through clothes: it is permissible if the clothes are not tight, but if the clothes reveal the shape, it is forbidden. The ruling applies to all adults, including slaves and disbelievers, but looking at a disbelieving woman's hair is allowed. The matn states that one who desires should not look except the judge, witness, and physician. The commentary explains that looking with desire is haram except for necessity. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a witness who knows he will desire may look when summoned to bear testimony; the correct view is that he may not, because someone else can be found. However, for adultery testimony, looking at the genitals is permitted despite desire to establish the hadd punishment, because the need is acute. This is a nuanced ruling that balances prohibition with necessity.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar): Looking with the eyes, with the legal nuance of gazing intentionally.
شهوة (shahwah): Sexual desire that arouses the person.
أجنبية (ajnabiyyah): A woman who is not a mahram (i.e., permissible to marry).
عورة (awrah): Body parts that must be covered; for men, between navel and knee; for women, the entire body except face and hands in prayer, but for looking, more restrictions apply.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition is it permissible for a witness to look at a non-mahram woman's face with desire?
A) Always, because testimony is obligatory.
B) Only if he intends to bear witness and no other man without desire is available.
C) Only if she is a disbeliever.
D) Never, because desire always prohibits looking.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling balances the prohibition of looking with desire against genuine necessities, permitting it for judges, witnesses, physicians, and in certain cases like establishing hadd, only when no alternative exists.

Touching Non-Mahram Women: Ruling, Exceptions, and Conditions
Rulings on touching non-mahram women, including exceptions for medical treatment, marriage, and handshake.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on touching a non-mahram woman, identify the exceptions (medical, marriage, handshake), and apply the conditions for permissible handshake based on the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
Touching a non-mahram woman is haram; exceptions require necessity and security from desire.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq builds on the previous lesson by specifying when touching a non-mahram woman becomes permissible. While looking is allowed for marriage, touching remains strictly prohibited except in cases of necessity like medical treatment or midwifery. A key discussion revolves around handshake (musafahah) with elderly women, where the preferred view requires both parties to be old and secure from desire.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Ruling
Determine the default hukm (legal ruling) for touching a non-mahram woman. Is it haram, makruh, or mubah? What is the basis?
The hadith threatens punishment, indicating haram.
2
Analyze the Exceptions
List the specific situations where touching or looking is permitted: medical treatment, marriage preparation, midwifery, and handshake with elderly/secure individuals. Note the conditions for each.
Medical necessity requires no female doctor; marriage permits looking but not touching.
3
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on Handshake
Two narrations exist: one requires both parties to be old and secure, another suffices with one. Understand the reasoning behind each and why the first is preferred.
The preferred view considers the risk of fitnah from the side that may still desire.
4
Apply the Principle of Necessity
See how the principle 'what is established for necessity is limited by its extent' (al-darurah tuqaddar bi qadariha) governs medical scenarios.
The physician covers all but the affected area and lowers gaze.

والطبيب إنما يجوز له ذلك إذا لم يوجد امرأة طبيبة فلو وجدت فلا يجوز له أن ينظر لأن نظر الجنس إلى الجنس أخف. وينبغي للطبيب أن يعلم امرأة إن أمكن وإن لم يكن ستر كل عضو منها سوى موضع الوجع ثم ينظر ويغض ببصره عن غير ذلك الموضع إن استطاع لأن ما ثبت للضرورة يتقدر بقدرها، وإذا أراد أن يتزوج امرأة فلا بأس أن ينظر إليها وإن خاف أن يشتهي لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «انظر إليها لأنه أحرى أن يدوم بينكما ولا يجوز له أن يمس وجهها ولا كفها وإن أمن الشهوة لوجود المحرم ولانعدام الضرورة وقال عليه الصلاة والسلام من مس كف امرأة ليس له فيها سبيل وضع على كفه جمر يوم القيامة» قال في التتارخانية : أصاب امرأة قرحة في موضع لا يحل للرجل النظر إليه، لم يوجد امرأة تداويها ولم يقدر أن يعلم امرأة تداويها يستر منها كل شيء إلا موضع القرحة ويغض بصره ما أمكن ويداويها. وفي المحيط أيضاً ويجوز للمرأة إذا كانت تولد أخرى أن تنظر إلى فرجها وأن تمس فرجها .اهـ. وقيدوا جواز النظر دون المس عند إرادة الزوج إذا كانت شابة تشتهى، وأما إذا كانت عجوزاً لا تشتهي فلا بأس بمصافحتها بدنها لانعدام خوف الفتنة. وعن أبي بكر رضي الله عنه أنه كان يصافح العجائز فإذا كان شيخاً يأمن على نفسه وعليها يحل له المصافحة، وإن يكونا لا يأمن عليها ولا على نفسه لا تحل له مصافحتها لما فيه من التعريض للفتنة. فحاصله أنه يشترط الجواز المس أن يكونا كبيرين مأمونين في رواية، وفي أخرى يكفي أن يكون أحدهما مأموناً كبيراً لأن أحدهما إذا كان لا يشتهي لا يكون اللمس سبباً للوقوع في الفتنة كالصغير. ووجه الأولى أن الشاب إذا كان لا يشتهي بمس العجوز فالعجوز تشتهي الشاب لأنها علمت بملاذ الجماع فيؤدي إلى الانتهاء من الجانبين وهو حرام بخلاف ما إذا كان أحدهما صغيراً لأنه لا يؤدي إلى الانتهاء من الجانبين لأن الكبير لايشتهي بمس الصغير، ولهذا إذا مات صغير أو صغيرة تغسله المرأة والرجل ما لم تبلغ حد الشهوة وكذا يجوز النظر إلى الصغير والصغيرة والمس إذا كان لا يشتهي.
And the physician is only permitted that if no female physician exists; if one exists, he is not permitted to look because looking of one gender at the same gender is lighter. It is desirable for the physician to teach a woman if possible; if not, he should cover every part of her except the place of pain, then look and lower his gaze from other places if he can, because what is established for necessity is limited by its extent. And when he wants to marry a woman, there is no harm in looking at her even if he fears he will desire, due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Look at her, for it is more likely to create love between you.' And he is not permitted to touch her face or palm even if safe from desire, due to the prohibition and lack of necessity. And he said, peace be upon him: 'Whoever touches the palm of a woman to whom he has no right, a piece of fire will be placed on his palm on the Day of Resurrection.' Al-Tatarakhaniyyah said: If a woman has an ulcer in a place where it is not lawful for a man to look, and no female doctor is found, and he cannot teach a woman, he should cover everything except the ulcer and lower his gaze as much as possible and treat her. In al-Muhit also: It is permissible for a woman who is midwifing another to look at her vulva and touch her vulva. End. They restricted the permissibility of looking without touching when intending marriage if the woman is young and desired; but if she is an old woman who is not desired, there is no harm in greeting her with handshake because there is no fear of fitnah. And from Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, that he used to shake hands with old women. So if a man is old and secure from himself and from her, it is permissible for him to shake hands. And if both are not secure from each other, it is not permissible for him to shake hands with her because of the exposure to fitnah. The sum is that the permissibility of touching requires that both be old and secure in one narration; in another, it suffices that one of them be old and secure because if one does not desire, the touch does not cause falling into fitnah, like a child. The first view is that if a young man does not desire by touching an old woman, the old woman desires the young man because she knows the pleasures of intercourse, leading to sin from both sides, which is haram. Unlike if one is a child, because it does not lead to sin from both sides, as the adult does not desire by touching a child. Hence, if a young boy or girl dies, a woman or man may wash them as long as they have not reached the age of desire. Similarly, it is permissible to look at a young boy or girl and touch if one does not desire.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage continues from the previous lesson, focusing on touching (مس) non-mahram women. The principle is that touching is more severe than looking. The physician is prioritized over a female physician only if necessary; otherwise, looking/touching by a same-gender doctor is lighter. The physician must minimize exposure. For marriage, looking is encouraged even if it causes desire, but touching is absolutely prohibited. A hadith warns of punishment for touching a non-mahram woman's hand. Medical necessity permits touching the affected area with strict conditions. Midwifery specifically allows looking and touching the vulva. The passage then discusses handshake (مصافحة) with non-mahram: it is permissible only when both parties are elderly and secure from desire. There is an intra-Hanafi difference: some say both must be secure, others say one suffices. The stronger view considers the psychological aspect: even if the man is secure, the woman may not be, leading to fitnah. For children who have not reached the age of desire, touching is permissible for washing the deceased. This balances the general prohibition with specific necessities.

Key Terms
مس (mass): Physical touch, including handshake or any contact.
مصافحة (musafahah): Handshake as a greeting between non-mahram individuals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a man shaking hands with a non-mahram elderly woman?
A) Always haram regardless of age.
B) Permissible only if both are old and secure from desire.
C) Permissible if the man is young but the woman is old.
D) Permissible only for the purpose of marriage.

Key Takeaway
Touching a non-mahram woman is haram; exceptions require strict necessity or security from desire, with the preferred Hanafi view requiring both parties to be elderly and secure for a handshake.

Preference of Studying Fiqh over Reciting Quran for a Faqih
A Hanafi ruling on comparative virtue of fiqh study over Quran recitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the comparative virtue of studying fiqh versus reciting the Quran for a faqih, and identify the underlying reasoning.

Core Idea
For a faqih, studying fiqh is more virtuous than reciting the Quran because it preserves and applies the religion.

This passage addresses a question about spiritual priorities for a scholar. Is it better for a faqih to recite the Quran or to study fiqh? The Hanafi position, as recorded by Ibn Nujaym in al-Bahr al-Raiq, asserts that engaging with fiqh texts is superior to even a night of prayer (qiyam al-layl). This lesson explores the reasoning behind this preference and its implications for a scholar's time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question and parties involved
The passage begins with a query: for a faqih, which is better – reciting Quran or studying fiqh? The response is attributed to Abu Bakr al-Faqih, citing Abu Muti' and Abu 'Asim.
Note the question is specific to a faqih, not a general Muslim.
2
Extract the ruling from Abu Muti''s statement
Abu Muti' states that 'looking into the books of our companions without hearing is better than standing in prayer for a night.' This directly compares studying fiqh (looking into books) with a supererogatory act of worship (qiyam al-layl).
'Without hearing' refers to reading without a teacher's oral transmission, showing the value of independent study.
3
Understand the analogy
The comparison is between two virtuous acts: studying fiqh and performing night prayer (which typically involves Quran recitation). The ruling elevates fiqh study above even this highly recommended act.
This is a relative preference, not a downgrading of qiyam al-layl.
4
Analyze Abu 'Asim's critique
Abu 'Asim says 'seeking hadith is the trade of the destitute' when one seeks hadith without fiqh. This emphasizes that fiqh gives meaning and application to revealed texts.
Hadith without fiqh can lead to misunderstanding; fiqh provides the framework.
5
Synthesize the overall Hanafi preference
Combine both statements: for a faqih, studying fiqh (including reading Hanafi works) is superior to individual Quran recitation because fiqh ensures correct practice and guidance of the community.
This is the mufta bihi position for a faqih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفقيه أبو بكر عن قراءة القرآن أهو أفضل للفقيه أم دراسة الفقه؟ قال : حكي عن الفقيه أبي مطيع أنه قال : النظر في كتب أصحابنا من غير سماع أفضل من قيام ليلة . وفي النوازل عن أبي عاصم أنه قال : طلب الأحاديث حرفة المفاليس يعني به إذا طلب الحديث ولم يطلب فقهاً.
Al-Faqih Abu Bakr [was asked] about reciting the Quran: is it more virtuous for a faqih or studying fiqh? He said: It is narrated from al-Faqih Abu Muti' that he said: Looking into the books of our companions without hearing is better than standing in prayer for a night. And in al-Nawazil from Abu 'Asim that he said: Seeking hadith is the trade of the destitute, meaning one who seeks hadith but does not seek fiqh.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage addresses the relative merit of two acts of worship for a scholar (faqih). The question is whether reciting the Quran or studying fiqh is better. The answer, attributed to Abu Muti', states that reading the books of the Hanafi companions (even without oral transmission) is superior to performing qiyam al-layl (night prayer) if it involves Quran recitation. Abu 'Asim further declares that seeking hadith without fiqh is the 'trade of the destitute' - a critique of those who collect hadith without understanding fiqh. The implication is that for a person qualified in fiqh, engaging in legal study is more beneficial than individual Quran recitation, because fiqh preserves the religion and guides practice.

Key Terms
الفقه (al-fiqh): Deep understanding of Islamic law; here specifically the study of Hanafi legal texts.
قيام ليلة (qiyam laylah): Voluntary night prayer, often involving long recitation of the Quran.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Muti', what is better than performing night prayer (qiyam al-layl) for a faqih?
A) Reciting the Quran
B) Looking into the books of the Hanafi scholars
C) Fasting every day
D) Giving charity

Key Takeaway
For a faqih, studying fiqh is more meritorious than reciting the Quran because it promotes understanding and application of divine law.

Rulings on Massaging (Ghamz) and Physical Contact
Fatwas on physical touch and modesty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on ghamz (massaging) for different body parts and relationships, and identify when it is permissible, disliked, or prohibited.

Core Idea
Touching another is regulated by modesty: shin is fine, thigh is disliked, and intention matters.

This lesson explores a series of fatwas on physical contact, specifically massaging (ghamz). The Hanafi school distinguishes between body parts and relationships, emphasizing modesty and intention. You will learn the detailed rulings for servants, parents, non-mahram women, and massaging a mother's belly and back over clothes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and relationship
Determine who is performing the touch (servant, man, woman) and the relationship (owner, parent, non-mahram, child-parent).
This sets the framework for permissibility.
2
Determine the body part
Differentiate between shin, thigh, belly, back, etc. Each has a ruling.
Shin is permissible, thigh is disliked, belly/back depend on intention.
3
Check for clothing barrier
Massaging over clothes is generally less severe than direct skin contact.
For non-mahram, over clothes is permissible if no fear of fitna.
4
Consider intention
The ruling can change based on whether the person holds the touched body part as sacred (like wine) or permissible.
This is highlighted in the fatwa about massaging the mother's belly.
5
Apply the ruling
Conclude: permissible, disliked (tanzihi), or prohibited based on the above factors.
Most rulings here are either permissible or disliked, not haram.

Chapter of Disliked Actions. That. And he was asked: one of them is that the servant should not wash his beard because it is disrespectful to the owner of the beard, and he should not massage his leg because it is disrespectful to the servant. Al-Faqih Abu Ja'far said: I heard the Shaykh al-Imam Abu Bakr say: There is no harm for a man to massage up to the shin, but it is disliked to massage the thigh and touch it from under the garment. Imam Abu Bakr used to say: There is no harm for a man to massage the leg of his parents, but he should not massage their thigh. In al-Sirajiyyah: There is no harm for a non-mahram woman to massage a man over clothes if there is no fear of temptation. In al-Tatimmah: Al-Khujandi was asked about a man who has a mother: is it permissible for him to massage her belly and back over clothes? He said: If he holds her believing her sanctity is like wine that a Muslim holds for another Muslim, it is not disliked; if he holds her believing it is permissible, like holding for a disbeliever, it is disliked. Anas ibn Anas was asked about people who wanted to rebel against their unjust ruler: is it permissible for them? He answered and said: If they are twelve thousand and have one word, it is permissible; if they are less than that, it is not permissible. Al-Faqih Abu Bakr was asked: is recitation of the Quran better for a jurist or studying fiqh? He said: It is narrated from al-Faqih Abu Muti' that he said: Looking at the books of our companions without hearing is better than standing in prayer for a night. In al-Nawazil from Abu 'Asim, he said: Seeking hadith is the craft of the destitute—meaning when one seeks hadith without seeking fiqh. In al-Nasafiyyah: A group of Turks, amirs, and others gathered in places of corruption. Shaykh al-Islam forbade them from evil but they did not desist. He summoned the muhtasib and some people from the gate of the Sayyid al-Imam al-Ajal to identify them and spill their wine. They went with a group of jurists, found some wine, spilled it, and put salt in some jars to turn it into vinegar. The Shaykh was informed and said: Do not leave them; break all the jars and spill what remains. And if they put salt in them, he said: It has been mentioned in 'Uyun al-Masa'il: Whoever spills the wine of Muslims, breaks their jars, and tears their wineskins, if done openly among Muslims by way of commanding good, there is no liability. He was asked about a group of Jews who bought a house and garden from Muslim houses in Egypt and made it a cemetery: should they be prevented? He said: No, because they own it, so they do as they wish like Muslims. And the narration in al-Mabsut is authentic that if the owner of a house raises a building, even if it blocks sunlight or wind, or he makes a hole in his wall or opens doors, he is not prevented even if it causes some harm to his neighbor, because he only acted within his own property. He was asked about two houses belonging to two men: the roof of one is higher than the other, and the water drainage from the higher roof goes to the lower. The owner of the lower roof wanted to raise his roof or build a second story on his roof: is it permissible? He said: Yes. In al-Tatimmah: I asked Abu Hamid about a man who has a farmland with high ground: is it permissible for him to dam the river for a day or part of a day without the consent of those downstream to irrigate his land? He said: Yes. He was asked about a man who builds on his own wall higher than before: does his neighbor have the right to prevent him? He said: No, even if it reaches the sky. Abu al-Fadl was asked about one who takes the land tax of the village and uses it to dig the great canal; they dig with their own spades without spending any of the land tax on digging, and there are strong people who do not dig nor send anyone: can he irrigate from it or not? He said: He is prevented from the water.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage presents a series of fatwa questions and answers from the Hanafi tradition, covering a wide range of practical issues from personal hygiene to public property rights. The author, likely the commentator Zayn al-Din ibn al-Humam, quotes earlier authorities such as al-Faqih Abu Ja'far, al-Shaykh al-Imam Abu Bakr, and others. The rulings follow the Hanafi school's principles: avoiding harm, respecting property, and maintaining public order. The first ruling concerns a servant's conduct: it is disliked for him to wash his beard (disrespect to the owner) or massage his leg (disrespect to the servant). Then there are detailed rulings on massaging body parts: permissible up to the shin, disliked on the thigh, permissible over clothing if no fitna, and specific rules for massaging parents. A notable ruling is that rebellion against an unjust ruler is only permissible if the group numbers twelve thousand and is united in opinion—otherwise it is not permissible. This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on avoiding anarchy. Regarding the priority of fiqh over Quran recitation, the passage reports that studying fiqh (looking at the books of the Hanafi school) is superior even to night prayer. This underscores the high value placed on fiqh in the Hanafi tradition. The section on commanding good and forbidding evil shows that spilling wine and breaking vessels is allowed without liability if done publicly to enforce Islamic law. However, for Jewish property owners, they are free to use their property as they wish, even for a cemetery, because they own it. The rulings on building and water rights emphasize the principle that a person may use his property freely even if it causes indirect harm to neighbors, as long as he does not exceed his ownership. This is a clear application of the Hanafi principle of 'al-tasarruf fi al-milk la yudman' (acting in one's own property incurs no liability). Finally, the water rights ruling states that those who do not contribute to digging the canal cannot irrigate from it, reflecting fairness in shared resources.

Key Terms
الغمز (al-ghamz): Pressing or massaging a body part, often with the hand.
الكراهية (al-karahiyya): The characteristic of being disliked (makruh) in Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the fatwa, a man massaging his mother's belly over clothes is:
A) Always permissible
B) Disliked if he holds it as permissible
C) Haram
D) Only permissible if she is sick

Key Takeaway
Physical touch rulings in Hanafi fiqh depend on body part, clothing, relationship, and intention, not just who touches whom.

Rebellion Against an Unjust Ruler
Fatwa on rebellion against unjust ruler

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for permissible rebellion against an unjust ruler and evaluate its application.

Core Idea
Rebellion is only allowed if the group is 12,000 strong and united; otherwise it is forbidden.

This lesson addresses a critical question: when is it permissible to rebel against an unjust ruler? The fatwa from Anas ibn Anas sets a numerical and unity condition. You will explore the rationale and limits of this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The question is about rebelling against a ruler who is unjust (jair).
This is a political fiqh matter.
2
State the condition for permissibility
The answer: if they are twelve thousand and their word is one (united).
This is a specific threshold.
3
Understand the threshold
12,000 is considered a strength that prevents anarchy and ensures success or at least deterrence.
Scholars interpret this as a number that guarantees effective opposition.
4
Consider the consequence
If less than that, rebellion is not permissible because it would cause more harm than good.
Avoiding fitna and bloodshed is prioritized.
5
Apply to modern contexts
This ruling reflects the Hanafi principle of avoiding rebellion unless there is strong likelihood of success without greater evil.
Modern applications consider similar thresholds.
